- **Request for Quotation & Supplier Quotation:** RFQ is typically an invitation; in TOSS we might simplify by directly entering Supplier Quotations. **Supplier Quotation** fields - linked RFQ (if any), supplier, quotation date, items with quoted rates and any terms (delivery time, etc.). Status (Open, Selected, Rejected).
- **Purchase Order:** Fields - Supplier, order date, expected date, items (with quantities, rates, amount, tax breakdown), terms and conditions, and status (Draft/Submitted/Completed). It also has a linkage to Material Requests or Quotation if applicable.
- **Purchase Receipt:** Fields - Supplier, receipt date, items received (with quantity received vs ordered, and any rejected quantity), warehouse, and if applicable batch numbers. Has a checkbox "Update Stock" (always true in this context) and status (Draft/Submitted).
- **Purchase Invoice:** Fields - Supplier, posting date, due date, items (with quantities, rates, amount which can be fetched from PO or receipt), taxes & charges (e.g., VAT), total, and status (Unpaid/Paid). Also includes a field for Supplier Invoice No. to record the vendor's reference. If this invoice is linked to a Purchase Receipt, the system can auto-reconcile quantities.
- **Payment Entry (for Purchase):** Fields - Supplier, payment date, mode (cash/bank), amount, and references (which invoice or advance it's for).

**User Interface & Experience:** The Buying module is designed to make replenishment easy rather than bureaucratic. On the TOSS dashboard, a shop owner might see a **"Low Stock Items"** indicator. By clicking it, they access a Reorder screen listing all items below their reorder level (with current stock and recommended order quantity). From there, Naledi (for example) could tick which items to order and click **"Make Purchase Order"**, and TOSS will draft a consolidated PO for those items to their default suppliers. This one-click replenishment is crucial for users who are not procurement experts.

When creating a **Purchase Order** manually, the form auto-suggests past order quantities and last prices: if you ordered 100 units of cement last time at R50, when you add cement this time, it might show R50 as rate and highlight "last ordered 2 weeks ago, 100 units" for context. This helps in negotiations and decisions. If multiple suppliers supply the item, TOSS could show a dropdown of supplier options or the last supplier used.

TOSS uses simple labels; for instance, instead of "Purchase Invoice", menus might show **"Supplier Bill"** to be more immediately understandable. The Purchase Invoice form might have a big toggle for "Is Paid?" if paying immediately, simplifying use for cash-on-delivery scenarios (checking it could auto-create the payment entry behind scenes).

On mobile, the forms for PO and receipt are streamlined: e.g., for a receipt, you select the PO, and it loads all items with ordered vs. you just adjust any that weren't fully delivered or are rejected. This reduces typing. There's also a feature to snap a photo of the supplier's delivery note or invoice using the device camera and attach it, so records are kept (useful for later dispute resolution).

**Offline Capabilities:** Procurement generally assumes some connectivity (to communicate with suppliers or update stock), but TOSS still supports offline use in parts of the workflow. For example, if a delivery is being checked in a storeroom with poor signal, the storekeeper can still open the last known PO on their device (cached) and enter the **Purchase Receipt** details offline. The data (new receipt, any rejections) will sync once back online, updating stock then. Creation of new POs offline is possible (say the owner at a trade show writes a quick PO on a tablet without net), and it will sync later and can then be emailed. The system queues outgoing RFQ emails until connectivity returns. One have to be cautious with offline ordering that pricing info might not be up-to-date without sync (TOSS caches the last known price lists per supplier).

Crucially, **Item and Supplier lists** are cached locally, so selecting products or suppliers offline is seamless. If there's an attempt to order an item that was newly added on server while the device was offline, the device wouldn't know - but that's an edge case. As a safeguard, TOSS might warn on sync if an item code on the PO was unrecognized (perhaps typed incorrectly offline), allowing correction.

**Example Use Case (Township Context):** _Thabo runs a small convenience shop. Every Sunday, he reviews stock to place orders for the week. Using the Buying module, he checks the_ _Reorder Suggestions_\*. TOSS shows: "Flour - 5 kg in stock (reorder level 20 kg) → Suggest order 15 kg; Cooking Oil - 2 bottles in stock (reorder 10) → Suggest order 8; Sugar - 0 in stock (reorder 10) → Suggest order 10" and so on. Thabo selects all three and clicks "Create Purchase Orders". Since Flour and Oil are from Supplier A, and Sugar from Supplier B in his item setup, TOSS actually creates two POs automatically: one for Supplier A listing flour and oil, and one for Supplier B for sugar.

He opens each PO draft, double-checks it, and then Submits. TOSS emails the POs to the respective suppliers (because their records have email contacts). Supplier A replies via phone confirming availability but at a slightly higher price for oil due to market changes. Thabo edits the PO in TOSS, updating the oil price (the system highlights the change and will reflect it on the invoice later).

On Monday, deliveries arrive. For Supplier A's delivery, he opens the PO on his phone, and presses **Receive Items**. The app brings up the Purchase Receipt screen with flour 15kg and oil 8 bottles pre-filled. He counts and finds everything correct. He submits the receipt, which immediately updates his stock for flour and oil (so sales people can see they're in stock now). Similarly, Supplier B delivers sugar 10 bags; he creates a Purchase Receipt from that PO as well.

Now he has two Purchase Invoices to record (the bills). For simplicity, Thabo opens the **Accounts Payable** section where he sees "2 Unpaid Supplier Bills" (the system can auto-create draft invoices from receipts). He opens each: they're already populated with the items and amounts from the PO/receipt. He notices the invoice from Supplier A also charged a R50 delivery fee that wasn't on the PO. He adds a line "Delivery Fee" (set as a non-stock expense item) to the Purchase Invoice. He then saves and submits it. This increases the amount payable accordingly.

Thabo has 30-day terms, so he doesn't pay immediately. TOSS now shows these invoices under "Bills to Pay". At month end, Thabo goes to the Payment interface, selects all open supplier invoices, and records a single payment for each that he is making via bank transfer. This closes them out. If he short-paid any (maybe disputing something), TOSS would keep the balance open.

Throughout this process, TOSS enforced that he couldn't receive more items than ordered, and when he entered that extra delivery fee, it properly categorized it as an expense. All the while, his inventory was kept correct - his staff saw sugar restocked in the system the moment he submitted the receipt, so they could start selling it. And by using the suggestions, Thabo ensured he ordered enough to meet demand but not overstock. The Buying module saved him time and prevented the common issue of forgetting to order something and then losing sales.\*

**SaaS 2.0 - AI & Insights:** The Buying module benefits from AI in several ways. **Automated reordering** is a primary feature: the AI monitors stock levels and sales velocity to generate smart reorder suggestions beyond simple minimum levels. For example, it might notice that certain items sell faster toward month-end (maybe when customers have salaries), so it suggests a larger reorder in that week and smaller in mid-month. It will also incorporate supplier lead times - if a supplier usually takes 1 week to deliver, AI can nudge the reorder earlier than a naive threshold trigger would[\[7\]](https://github.com/Toss-Online-Services/TossErp/blob/10c8061dab87e2b12429c455fe4113e62a69f8e0/.cursor/rules/toss.mdc#L110-L118)[\[28\]](https://github.com/Toss-Online-Services/TossErp/blob/10c8061dab87e2b12429c455fe4113e62a69f8e0/.cursor/rules/toss.mdc#L114-L117). Essentially, it becomes an intelligent planner: _"Based on current stock and sales trend, order 5 more crates of soda now to avoid running out by Friday."_

AI also keeps an eye on **supplier performance and pricing**. It can analyze purchase history to alert if a supplier's prices have crept up significantly or if they frequently deliver short. For example, _"Supplier B has increased sugar price 5% above average market price_[_\[27\]_](https://github.com/Toss-Online-Services/TossErp/blob/10c8061dab87e2b12429c455fe4113e62a69f8e0/.cursor/rules/toss.mdc#L110-L115)_. You might want to compare alternatives."_ If multiple suppliers exist for an item, AI could recommend splitting orders for best pricing or reliability.

For payment management, AI might watch cash flow and suggest optimal timing for purchases: _"Your cash is low and you have a big payment due next week; consider deferring non-urgent purchases."_ Conversely, it could spot opportunities like early payment discounts: _"Supplier A offers 2% off for early payment - paying now could save R100 (and your cash balance is healthy)."_

Another aspect is **inventory quality**: AI can tie into quality data (if available) to suggest not buying from suppliers who had quality issues, or highlight if an item often gets returned (maybe indicating a supplier problem).

During purchase order entry, AI can assist by suggesting likely order quantities based on past frequency (_"You typically buy 10 bottles of oil monthly, last purchase was 4 weeks ago"_) and even flagging if you're ordering an abnormal amount relative to sales (_"This order is 3x your usual quantity for rice - check if this is intended"_). This kind of insight helps avoid both overstocking and under-ordering.

**Collaborative Network Features:** On the network level, the Buying module unlocks one of the network's biggest advantages: **group purchasing power**. Multiple small businesses can combine their orders to negotiate bulk rates from suppliers. TOSS supports this by enabling **Group Purchase Orders**. For instance, five independent spaza shops in the network could agree to do a joint procurement of a wholesale lot of cooking oil. Through the collaborative layer, they can use TOSS to create a single **Group Purchase Order** that aggregates all their needs (100 bottles total, where each shop's contribution is marked)[\[8\]](https://github.com/Toss-Online-Services/TossErp/blob/10c8061dab87e2b12429c455fe4113e62a69f8e0/.cursor/rules/toss.mdc#L120-L124). This PO is then sent to the supplier with the network's collective branding or via a central coordinating entity. When the goods arrive, TOSS will split the received quantities into each shop's individual Purchase Receipts (e.g., Shop A gets 20, Shop B 30, etc., summing to the 100) and their respective payables. Each shop benefits from the lower bulk price, and the network ensures fair distribution and accounting of who owes what.

The Collaborative Network also allows **supplier relationship management** at a group level. The network might maintain a shared supplier rating system within TOSS - if one member logs a serious late delivery or bad quality from a supplier, that info can be optionally shared (with moderation) to other members, so everyone is aware. This fosters a sort of community-reviewed supplier list, pushing suppliers to maintain good service to not lose the entire network's business. Additionally, the network could designate certain suppliers as preferred or negotiate contract prices on behalf of all members. Those negotiated price lists can be loaded into TOSS so that every member's Buying module automatically uses the better rates.

Another feature is **shared warehouse or logistics**: if the network jointly orders in bulk, they might bring goods into a central warehouse then distribute. TOSS can facilitate this by, for example, creating an internal **Transfer Order** from the central warehouse to each shop after the group purchase is received (essentially treating the network's hub as a supplier to the shops with zero markup). The system can trigger these transfers once the group PO is received, making the process seamless for members.

Through collaboration, even tasks like procurement planning can be shared. One experienced member or a hired procurement officer could oversee generating purchase plans for common commodities (like staple foods, airtime stock, etc.) and TOSS can present that plan to each member for approval. This way, less experienced shop owners get the benefit of expert planning.

In summary, the Buying module in a network turns the many small orders into one powerful order[\[8\]](https://github.com/Toss-Online-Services/TossErp/blob/10c8061dab87e2b12429c455fe4113e62a69f8e0/.cursor/rules/toss.mdc#L120-L124), reduces costs via bulk discounts, and strengthens negotiating power and information symmetry with suppliers - leveling the playing field between tiny township businesses and large retailers.

## CRM (Customer Relationship Management Module)

**Functional Overview & Purpose:** The CRM module focuses on managing leads, customer interactions, and sales opportunities, serving as the pre-sales and client relationship hub that feeds into the Selling module. It helps businesses **capture leads, nurture opportunities, and maintain customer relationships** in an organized way[\[13\]](https://docs.frappe.io/erpnext/v13/user/manual/en/CRM#:~:text=The%20CRM%20module%20in%20ERPNext,interactions%20and%20streamline%20sales%20processes). For township and rural businesses, CRM ensures that potential customers (whether walk-ins showing interest, referrals, or inquiries via phone/WhatsApp) are recorded and followed up, so no opportunity is lost due to forgetfulness or disorganization. It also centralizes customer contact info and interaction history, which is valuable for building loyalty and repeat sales.

**Core Workflows:**

- **Lead Capture:** Record new **Leads** (potential customers or inquiries) with basic info: name, contact number, maybe how they heard about the business (referral source). A lead could be someone who showed interest in a product but hasn't bought yet, or someone who filled a "contact us" form on the website. TOSS CRM provides a quick Lead form to fill in these details[\[31\]](https://docs.frappe.io/erpnext/v13/user/manual/en/CRM#:~:text=,to%20ensure%20effective%20customer%20engagement).
- **Lead Qualification & Follow-ups:** Set the status of leads (e.g., Open, Contacted, Converted, or Lost). Schedule follow-up actions for each lead - e.g., a task or reminder to call them back next week. The CRM can list all leads needing action on a given day. It also allows logging call notes or meeting notes, so each lead's timeline shows every touchpoint (call made, WhatsApp sent, etc.).
- **Opportunity Management:** When a lead expresses concrete interest (say requests a quote or shows intent to buy), convert that lead into an **Opportunity**. An Opportunity records the potential deal: which product or service they're interested in, estimated value, and expected closing date[\[31\]](https://docs.frappe.io/erpnext/v13/user/manual/en/CRM#:~:text=,to%20ensure%20effective%20customer%20engagement). It goes through stages (e.g., Initial Inquiry → Quotation Sent → Negotiation → Won/Lost).
- **Quotation & Conversion:** The CRM links seamlessly to Selling - you can create a **Quotation** directly from an Opportunity once details are firm. That quotation then moves through the sales cycle as described in Selling. The CRM Opportunity gets updated when a quote is sent or an order is won, so you can analyze conversion rates.
- **Customer Management:** Once a lead or opportunity is won, it becomes a **Customer** in the system (if not already). The CRM module then overlaps with customer service: it holds contact details (phone, email), multiple addresses, and a communications log for each customer[\[85\]](https://docs.frappe.io/erpnext/v13/user/manual/en/CRM#:~:text=forecast%20potential%20revenue.%20,customer%20behavior%2C%20and%20CRM%20effectiveness). It also shows all deals (orders, invoices) linked to that customer, effectively providing a 360° view.
- **Activity Tracking:** Manage all interactions with both leads and existing customers. This includes scheduling calls or visits (which can integrate with a calendar), sending emails (if email is set up, TOSS can send and capture replies), and capturing any customer requests or complaints to hand off to Support if needed.
- **Reporting & Analytics:** CRM provides simple reports like Lead Funnel (how many leads converted to opportunities and then to sales), or lists of customers by last interaction date (to identify those who haven't been contacted recently). This helps prioritize outreach.

**Business Rules & Validation:**

- **Lead De-duplication:** When entering a new lead, the system can warn if a similar contact already exists (by matching phone or email). This avoids duplicate entries for the same person, ensuring all info is in one place.
- **Mandatory Follow-up:** Optionally, the business might enforce that every new lead must have a next follow-up date set. This prevents leads from languishing without attention. TOSS can prompt: "Set a follow-up reminder" when saving a lead.
- **Opportunity Probability & Forecast:** Each opportunity can have a probability of closing (maybe auto-set by stage or manual). The system ensures total forecast values are calculated, but this is more advisory. It might, however, flag opportunities with closing dates that have passed without update.
- **Customer Status:** If a customer is marked inactive or blacklisted (perhaps due to bad debt), the CRM could warn or block new opportunities or quotes for them without managerial override.
- **Data Privacy:** Keep customer personal info secure - for example, if multiple staff use the system, one might restrict who can export or see full contact lists (though in a small biz, likely one user or a couple owners have access anyway).
- **Lead Aging:** If a lead hasn't been contacted in, say, 14 days, the system might escalate it as "cold" or change status. This can be configured or simply managed via reports.

**Required Data Models & Key Fields:**

- **Lead:** Fields - Lead Name, Contact info (phone, email), Source (how lead was found: referral, web, walk-in, etc.), Interested Item/Service (optional quick note), Lead Owner (if multiple employees handling leads), Status (Open, Contacted, Converted, Do Not Contact). May also capture Company name if it's a business lead.
- **Opportunity:** Fields - linked Customer/Lead, Opportunity Type (sales, project, etc.), Items of interest (item and quantity, or a general description of need), Estimated value, Expected closing date, Stage (Qualification, Proposal, etc.), Probability (%), and Opportunity Owner. Could also link to a specific Quotation when one is made.
- **Customer:** (Shared with Selling) - aside from master data, used in CRM to store segment info or loyalty program ID, etc.
- **Contact & Address:** Separate DocTypes that can link to Lead or Customer. Contact fields - name, phone, mobile, email. Address fields - address lines, city, etc., plus whether billing/shipping for customers.
- **Communication/Interaction:** The system logs communications as timeline entries. These may not be a separate doctype user fills (except for sending emails or adding a comment), but behind the scenes, a **Communication** entity stores the content of an email or a call log with a link to Lead/Customer.
- **Todo/Reminder:** Internally tasks or events for follow-ups. Possibly integrated with a calendar. Fields - description, due date, linked Lead/Opportunity, assigned to user.
- **Campaign:** If doing marketing, one could tag leads or opportunities with a Campaign name (e.g., "Easter Promo 2025"), to evaluate campaign effectiveness. Campaign could be its own doctype with start/end and type (SMS blast, radio ad, etc.).

**User Interface & Experience:** The CRM views are centered around people and companies. For instance, the app might have a **Leads list** that shows each lead's name, status, and a colored indicator if a follow-up is due or overdue. Tapping a lead opens a **Lead Details** page showing contact info at top and a timeline of interactions (e.g., "Jan 5: Called - interested in pricing, follow-up on Jan 10"). There's a prominent button to **"Convert to Opportunity"** or **"Create Quotation"** if the lead becomes hot.

The **Opportunity Kanban** is a useful UI feature: opportunities can be visualized in columns by stage (New, Quotation Sent, Negotiation, Won, Lost). On a tablet or laptop, user can drag an opportunity card from one stage to another as it progresses. On mobile, perhaps a dropdown to change stage. This gives a quick overview of the sales pipeline.

CRM has a **Today's Agenda** sort of view: a combined list of all tasks, calls, and follow-ups due today or soon. So when a business owner starts the day, they might see: "Call Ms. Dlamini (lead) - due today", "Follow up quote QUO-0004 with Joe (opportunity) - due yesterday (overdue)", etc. Each entry can be tapped to update or mark complete. This ensures no customer is forgotten.

When creating a communication (like sending an email or SMS from within TOSS), the UI is simple: select a template or type a message, and send. The text is automatically logged under that contact's timeline. For emails, if integrated, replies could route back into the timeline as well.

In terms of language, TOSS CRM uses friendly terms: e.g., "New Friend" or "New Contact" could be synonyms for leads in some localized versions (or at least tooltips). And instead of "Opportunity", sometimes "Deal" or "Potential Sale" might be used in the UI, which is more intuitive for small business users. We assume a bit of business understanding though, so "Lead" and "Opportunity" can be kept if explained once.

**Offline Capabilities:** Much of CRM can work offline as well. If a salesperson is out in the field meeting people, they can add new leads offline on their phone. Those leads will sync to the central system later. The app caches recent leads, opportunities, and customers, so one can pull up a customer's phone number or address offline when visiting them. Logging an interaction (say, after a meeting, writing summary notes) can be done offline too - it will timestamp and sync later.

One limitation offline is sending communications - if you draft an email offline, the app will queue it and send once connected (ensuring no leads get an email at a random time, the system might only send during business hours or upon next connection). Also, if multiple team members handle CRM, offline use by one might lead to some sync conflicts like two people editing the same lead details - but given likely usage, this is minimal.

**Example Use Case (Township Context):** _Sibusiso offers solar panel installations in rural areas. At a local farmers' market, he meets numerous people interested in solar solutions. Using TOSS CRM on his phone, he quickly adds each_ _Lead_\*: e.g., "Emily Nkosi - 5km out of town, wants quote for borehole solar pump - 082xxxx" and "Green Village Co-op - interested in solar for community center - 010xxxx". He assigns a follow-up date for each next week.

Back in the office (or home), he opens TOSS on his laptop. In the **Leads** view, he sees those new entries. He calls Emily on Monday as scheduled (the app reminded him that morning). In the lead's timeline, he clicks "Log Call" and notes "Discussed needs, site visit set for Wed". He also moves her status to **Contacted**. After the site visit Wednesday, he realizes it's a serious prospect: he converts Emily from Lead to **Opportunity** right in the app, specifying "Solar Pump 1.5kW" as the item of interest and an estimated value of R30k. He then uses TOSS Selling to craft a **Quotation** and sends it to Emily.

Meanwhile, the **Green Village Co-op** lead emailed some specifications. Through TOSS's integrated email (or by logging manually), Sibusiso records that on their lead timeline. He schedules a meeting. That lead might take longer, but the system ensures he won't forget it - it's always listed in his open leads.

Emily accepts the quote - Sibusiso marks the Opportunity as **Won** (which also creates her as a Customer in the system). The CRM automatically records that outcome in funnel analysis. For Green Village, suppose they haven't decided for months - the system will periodically remind Sibusiso to touch base or eventually mark it inactive if cold.

Sibusiso also uses CRM for existing customers: e.g., he sets reminders to call each past client 6 months after install for a check-in (potential upsell of batteries or maintenance service). These show up in his ToDo list. When one customer, John, says he's interested in expanding his system, Sibusiso logs an **Opportunity** under John's customer record (skipping lead since John is already a customer). Now he can manage that potential repeat sale through the pipeline too.

All this data - how many leads he got, from where (say, he tags some as "Market Booth" vs "Facebook Ad"), how many converted - is available in CRM reports. He learns that 50% of the leads from market booth became opportunities, a great conversion, so he decides to do it again next month. CRM helped him systematically build and convert interest into revenue, rather than relying on scraps of paper or memory.\*

**SaaS 2.0 - AI & Insights:** AI in the CRM module serves as a virtual sales assistant and customer analyst. It can automatically prioritize leads by analyzing which are most likely to convert. For example, based on past data, it might score leads who provided detailed project info higher than those who just said "call me," and prompt Sibusiso to focus on the high-score leads first (a form of lead scoring). It also notices patterns - e.g., leads from certain sources or regions converting better - and will surface those insights: _"Leads from the weekly market have a 60% conversion rate_[_\[31\]_](https://docs.frappe.io/erpnext/v13/user/manual/en/CRM#:~:text=,to%20ensure%20effective%20customer%20engagement)_, higher than other sources. Consider investing more in that channel."_

For ongoing opportunities, the AI looks at the pipeline and can forecast sales. It might say: _"You have 5 open deals worth R50k likely closing this month (estimated from stage probabilities)."_ If it sees a lot of deals stuck in one stage for too long (e.g., quotations sent but no update in 30 days), it will gently remind: _"3 opportunities have not been updated in over a month. You may want to follow up or close them."_

The AI can also suggest **next best actions** for each opportunity. For instance: _"Opportunity Green Village Co-op has been in Negotiation for 2 weeks. Suggest offering a small discount or a financing plan to close the deal."_ It could base this on what has worked for similar past customers (pattern recognition).

When dealing with existing customers, AI watches their purchase history and interactions. It might alert: _"Customer John's energy usage is up - recommend upsell a battery upgrade."_ Or if a regular customer hasn't bought in a while: _"Client XYZ hasn't ordered in 3 months (normally monthly). Consider reaching out with a promotion."_ This ties CRM to Selling and even Support, as it uses holistic data to prompt retention moves.

Moreover, AI can help draft personalized communications. For example, when sending a follow-up email to a lead, it might suggest a tailored message: _"Hi Emily, since we last spoke, I thought you'd be interested in our new pump model that fits borehole setups like yours..."_ - saving Sibusiso time crafting emails while adding a professional touch.

**Collaborative Network Features:** In a network context, CRM can be extended to **referral sharing and collective customer insights**. For example, network members could agree to refer leads to each other: if one gets an inquiry they cannot service (out of their area or expertise), they log it in TOSS and flag it as available for referral. Other network members see these shared leads (perhaps in a special communal lead pool) and can pick them up. The system can then track referral fees or simply note the source. This way, a lead never goes to waste - the network increases the chance someone serves that customer, keeping business within the community.

The network might also maintain a **shared customer blacklist** for problematic clients (with caution and legal considerations). If a customer defrauds one member (e.g., takes goods on credit and vanishes), that member could mark them, and optionally that warning is visible to others so they don't also extend credit. Conversely, a shared **loyalty program** across the network means CRM modules of different businesses might share some customer data (like a loyalty ID and points). If a customer shops at multiple network stores, their profile (name, phone) could be recognized across, with permission, so their points accumulate network-wide. TOSS could handle this by a central loyalty service that CRM of each tenant hooks into.

The network can also aggregate CRM metrics for collective insight. Without revealing individual customer info, it could show, say, common trends: _"There's growing demand for solar pumps in outskirt villages - 3 members reported similar leads."_ This can encourage members to tailor offerings accordingly or do joint marketing.

Additionally, joint marketing campaigns can be run through the network's CRM collaboration. The network could initiate a campaign (e.g., an SMS blast or flyer drop across the township) and distribute the leads it generates among members fairly (maybe by territory). TOSS could tag leads by campaign and origin, so when they flow in via a central point (like the network's website or hotline), they are routed to the appropriate local member's CRM.

Privacy is key: any sharing of customer info in the network must be consent-based and opt-in by members. TOSS provides the tools (like flags to share a lead or a customer across tenants) but the network governance decides the rules. Used wisely, these CRM collaboration features mean small businesses can act like a larger unified enterprise in customer acquisition and service - referring business to each other, recognizing customers across shops (with loyalty or just better service), and collectively learning about their customer base to improve offerings.

## Stock (Inventory Management Module)

**Functional Overview & Purpose:** The Stock (Inventory) module enables businesses to **track and control their inventory** of products and materials across one or multiple locations. It maintains item master data, current stock levels, and records every stock movement (stock in, stock out, transfers) with appropriate valuation. This ensures that small businesses know exactly what they have in stock, what is running low, and can avoid losses due to stockouts or overstocking[\[23\]](https://docs.frappe.io/erpnext/v12/user/manual/en/stock#:~:text=The%20Stock%20or%20Inventory%20module,in%20ERPNext%20helps%20you%20to). It's tightly integrated with Purchasing, Selling, and Accounting: purchase receipts add stock, sales/deliveries reduce stock, and it provides real-time inventory values for financials.

**Core Workflows:**

- **Item Catalog & Units:** Define all **Items** that are kept in stock with a unique code and key details (unit of measure, default buying/selling rate, whether it's batch-managed or has serial numbers, etc.). Organize items by groups or categories for easier reporting. For example, in a spaza shop, item groups might be Beverages, Snacks, Cleaning Supplies, etc.
- **Stock In (Receipt):** Whenever new stock comes in, either from a supplier (via **Purchase Receipt**) or other sources (e.g., an opening stock entry, or manufacturing output), it's recorded. This increases the quantity on hand in the specified warehouse. The system logs the date, source document (like PO or production batch), and who received it.
- **Stock Out (Delivery/Consumption):** When stock leaves - typically through a **Delivery Note** for a sale, or through an **Stock Adjustment** for things like wastage, spoilage, or giving samples - it's recorded and deducts from on-hand quantity. All such movements can be captured by reason codes (sold, damaged, expired, etc.).
- **Stock Transfers:** If a business has multiple locations or even just storage vs. storefront, use **Stock Transfer** entries to move goods from one warehouse to another (e.g., from back storage to the shop floor). TOSS will decrease stock in source and increase in destination accordingly, ensuring total inventory remains same.
- **Stock Reconciliation (Physical Count):** Periodically, perform a **stock take**. Using TOSS, generate a list of items and count them in person. Then use a **Stock Reconciliation** entry to adjust the system counts to match physical counts, accounting for any discrepancies (shrinkage or unrecorded receipts). This posts any difference to an appropriate expense (inventory loss) or adjustment account.
- **Batch and Serial Number Tracking:** For items that need it (e.g., medicine with expiry dates or electronics with unique serials), the module allows assigning incoming stock to **Batch numbers** (with manufacturing/expiry dates) or **Serial numbers** (one per unit). All out movements then require specifying which batch/serial is being delivered or consumed. This helps in quality control and warranty tracking.
- **Inventory Valuation & Ledger:** Behind the scenes, every stock move updates the **Stock Ledger** (quantities per item per warehouse over time) and the **Stock Balance**. The module also calculates inventory value using methods like FIFO or Moving Average by default. For accounting, it generates accounting entries for stock movements if integrated with perpetual inventory (inventory account and cost of goods sold) so that the books always match the physical stock value[\[86\]](https://docs.frappe.io/erpnext/v12/user/manual/en/stock#:~:text=,Provide%20valuation%20of%20each%20product).
- **Alerts & Reports:** The module can trigger alerts for low stock (when falling below re-order level as noted in item). There are also reports like Stock Balance (current qty and value of each item), Stock Ledger (detailed movements log), and Ageing (which batches are nearing expiry or items not moving for long).

**Business Rules & Validation:**

- **No Negative Stock (configurable):** By default, TOSS will prevent transactions from taking an item's stock below zero unless explicitly allowed. For instance, you can't deliver 5 units if only 3 are in stock - it will throw an error. (Some businesses allow "virtual negative stock" if they want to invoice even if stock not updated yet, but it's an option).
- **First-In-First-Out (FIFO):** If using batch/expiry, or if configured for FIFO costing, the system will consume older stock first by default when you do not specify a batch (or it can auto-suggest which batch to use). This ensures proper rotation (especially for perishables, sell oldest first) and correct cost calculation.
- **Mandatory Fields:** On stock entries, fields like Item, Quantity, and Warehouse are mandatory. If the item has serial numbers, you must provide those serials equal to the quantity. If batched, you must pick a batch or create a new one when receiving.
- **Stock Freeze (Period Lock):** Optionally, an admin can "freeze" stock books as of a certain date (say year-end) so that no backdated entries can be made before that date without permission. This preserves historical inventory integrity after financial closing.
- **Valuation Consistency:** Every stock entry that affects value (buying, selling, adjustment) must have a proper rate/valuation. If an item is received without a purchase rate (like opening balance load), the system requires input of a value. If inventory is managed in multiple currencies or has additional costs, the system ensures conversion and inclusion of those costs if directed.
- **Integration Hooks:** When a Delivery Note is submitted, the system automatically makes the stock deduction and (if perpetual inventory) triggers the accounting for COGS at that moment[\[87\]](https://docs.frappe.io/erpnext/v12/user/manual/en/stock#:~:text=,product). Similarly, a Purchase Receipt triggers an increase and provisional asset of stock. These are enforced to keep sub-ledger and GL in sync.
- **Expiration Control:** For batch-tracked items, TOSS can disallow selling if expired (if configured). At minimum it flags warnings for near-expiry items in reports. Also, during stock reconciliation, if user marks some expired and removes them, those go out as scrap adjustments.
- **UOM Conversions:** If items are purchased in one unit (case of 24) and sold in another (bottles), the system maintains a conversion factor. It will validate that transactions use allowed UOMs and convert stock correctly (e.g., buying 1 case adds 24 bottles to stock).

**Required Data Models & Key Fields:**

- **Item:** (shared with Selling/Buying) - Fields relevant to stock: Item Code, Item Name, Stock UOM, whether maintains stock, default warehouse, re-order level, valuation method (if item-specific), whether batch/serial tracking is enabled, and an Item Group classification.
- **Warehouse:** Represents a stock location (could be a physical room or just a virtual partition). Hierarchical (a store can have sub-warehouses like "Front Shelf" vs "Back Store" if needed). Key fields: Warehouse Name, Parent (for hierarchy), and possibly an address or location description.
- **Stock Ledger Entry:** Internal table logging every quantity change with: Item, Warehouse, Date/Time, Voucher Type (e.g., Delivery Note), Voucher No, Qty In, Qty Out, Balance Qty, Valuation Rate, Balance Value after the entry[\[23\]](https://docs.frappe.io/erpnext/v12/user/manual/en/stock#:~:text=The%20Stock%20or%20Inventory%20module,in%20ERPNext%20helps%20you%20to). (This is more backend, surfaced via reports).
- **Stock Entry:** General doctype for stock movements that are not sales or purchase. It has a purpose (Material Issue, Material Receipt, Material Transfer, Repack, etc.). Fields: Purpose, From Warehouse (if issue/transfer), To Warehouse (if receipt/transfer), Items table (Item, Qty, Source and Target warehouses per row if split, Serial/Batch details, and maybe an Expense Account for issues). This is used for adjustments and transfers.
- **Batch:** Fields - Batch ID, Item, Manufacture Date, Expiry Date, and current quantity (computed as sum of that batch across warehouses, or separate per warehouse in some implementations). Batches are created on purchase receipt or manufacturing output.
- **Serial No:** Fields - Serial Number, Item, Warehouse, Status (Available/Delivered/Returned/etc.), and warranty expiry or other custom fields if needed. A serial record moves with stock movements.
- **Stock Reconciliation:** A document to adjust stock to a physical count. Fields: Posting date, list of items with current recounted quantity, and system computes difference. On submission, it posts those differences to ledger and relevant accounts.
- **Reorder Level (in Item or separate):** Fields - for each item (and optionally per warehouse) a minimum level and a reorder quantity. Could be stored in Item or a child doctype like "Item Reorder". TOSS uses these for suggestions.

**User Interface & Experience:** The Stock module in TOSS often works behind the scenes (adjusting to sales/purchases), but when used directly, it provides clear, action-oriented interfaces. For example, an **Inventory Dashboard** might show at a glance: Total Stock Value, Number of Items in stock, and Alerts like "3 items below re-order level"[\[23\]](https://docs.frappe.io/erpnext/v12/user/manual/en/stock#:~:text=The%20Stock%20or%20Inventory%20module,in%20ERPNext%20helps%20you%20to). Tapping an alert could take the user to a filtered list of those items with buttons to "Reorder" (linking to Buying) or to view details.

The **Item Master** form is streamlined; many fields like valuation method or accounts might be hidden under an "Advanced" section for simplicity, letting the user mostly fill in name, default price, and stock tracking options. For items with serial numbers, the item form can show a list of all serials and their status for quick reference.

Day-to-day stock operations often happen in the background via other modules (e.g., selling auto issues stock), but for manual tasks: - The **Stock Transfer** UI can present a simple flow: "From Warehouse \[dropdown\] → To Warehouse \[dropdown\]", then a table to scan or select items and quantities to move. This could be made even simpler by supporting a barcode scan mode where a staff scans each item leaving the storeroom; the app increments those in a list, then they confirm transfer to front. - The **Stock Adjustment** (Material Issue/Receipt) UI might ask for a reason: e.g., "Write-off / Shrinkage" or "New stock found". For a write-off, it might prompt for a short note ("e.g., broken bottles") to encourage good record-keeping. - There's also a **Quick Stock Balance Lookup**: in TOSS, perhaps a search bar where typing an item name shows current stock on hand (and maybe incoming POs). This is useful when a customer asks if something is in stock or to decide to reorder. This quick lookup can be accessible from selling screens too.

On mobile, scanning is a theme: TOSS could allow using the device camera as a barcode scanner in stock entry forms, accelerating counts or transfers. The interface uses large, easy buttons for actions like "Receive Stock" or "Count Stock" to cater to less tech-savvy staff.

**Offline Capabilities:** Inventory data is cached to allow basic lookup offline - e.g., a salesperson can see current stock even if connection drops (from the last sync). Doing a **Stock Count** in a warehouse with no connectivity is a prime offline scenario: the staff can use TOSS offline to mark down quantities of each item they count. The app would store these and later, when online, convert it into a Stock Reconciliation entry to adjust records. If a tablet is used, they might do the whole reconciliation offline and sync the final numbers.

Likewise, if a delivery is being made offline (POS sale or manual issue for charity, etc.), the stock will decrement in the local app and mark pending sync. TOSS's sync logic ensures FIFO or batch selection is consistent - e.g., it might reserve specific batch numbers for an offline delivery based on what the local app last knew as available, and when syncing if it finds a discrepancy (maybe that batch was consumed by another user), it will intelligently adjust or flag for user intervention.

**Example Use Case (Township Context):** _Maria runs a small clothing boutique. Every few weeks, she does a full inventory count. Traditionally this was pen-and-paper, but with TOSS Stock module on her phone, it's become easier. One Sunday when the shop is closed, she opens TOSS and chooses_ _Stock Take Mode_\* for her "Main Shop" warehouse. As she scans the barcode of each clothing item on the rack, the app matches it to an item and prompts for quantity. For unique pieces, quantity is 1; for common items like socks, she scans a sock tag and enters 30 (pairs). She works section by section. Because her internet is spotty inside the container that houses her boutique, the app is working offline. It's saving her count as she goes.

By the end, Maria has counted everything. She reviews the offline data: the app shows her count vs. what the system expected for each item, highlighting differences. For example, it expected 32 pairs of socks but she counted 30. She confirms to create adjustments for the differences. Later that day when she gets network, she opens TOSS and it syncs the **Stock Reconciliation**: inventory levels for socks are brought down by 2, and those 2 are posted as an "Inventory Loss (Shrinkage)" expense. Now her system stock matches reality.

In daily operation, Maria uses the Stock module mostly indirectly. When she sells an item via POS, TOSS automatically reduces the stock. If an item gets damaged (say a shirt gets a stain), she uses the **Material Issue** function: selects "Damaged Goods" reason, the item, and quantity 1 - this immediately writes off that piece from stock and records the loss. When new inventory arrives from her suppliers, she either uses **Purchase Receipt** (in Buying) which updates stock, or if it's consignment stock without a PO, she can directly do a **Material Receipt** in Stock module for those items with a note "Consignment from Supplier X".

Maria also appreciates the low-stock alerts: she set re-order levels on fast-moving items. If ladies' t-shirts drop below 5, TOSS highlights it. One day, a customer asks for an out-of-stock design; using her phone, Maria quickly checks TOSS and sees it's indeed 0 in stock, but also sees she has 10 on order coming next week (because a Purchase Order in the system is pending) - she can confidently inform the customer when to come back[\[88\]](https://docs.frappe.io/erpnext/v12/user/manual/en/stock#:~:text=The%20Stock%20module%20is%20tightly,Purchase%2C%20Manufacturing%20and%20Accounting%20modules). This kind of information at her fingertips improves her professionalism and reduces missed sales.

The Stock reports in TOSS also taught Maria that some products just don't move. By running an **Ageing report**, she found certain dress styles sitting for 6 months. She decided to discount them to free up cash. Without TOSS tracking each item's age and quantity, she might have overlooked those old items. Now her inventory is leaner and more tuned to what customers want.\*

**SaaS 2.0 - AI & Insights:** AI supercharges the Stock module by analyzing usage patterns and optimizing inventory management. One key feature is **demand forecasting**: the AI looks at past sales trends (seasonality, growth) and suggests how much stock to hold or reorder. For example, it might tell Maria: _"Based on sales in the last 3 months, you should keep at least 40 pairs of socks in stock (current 30 is likely to stockout)_[_\[37\]_](https://github.com/Toss-Online-Services/TossErp/blob/10c8061dab87e2b12429c455fe4113e62a69f8e0/toss-web/server/data/modules.ts#L510-L514)_."_ Conversely, for slow-movers: _"Dresses Category B has 20 pieces in stock but average monthly sales are 2 - consider reducing stock or running a promotion."_

AI-driven **reorder suggestions** go beyond static reorder levels. It can dynamically adjust reorder points by learning patterns like higher demand on weekends or specific events. It also takes into account lead time: _"Item X takes 2 weeks to deliver and you sell 5/week, so reorder when you hit 10 left (instead of the default 5) to avoid stockout."_ These suggestions appear in the Reorder report or as alerts[\[37\]](https://github.com/Toss-Online-Services/TossErp/blob/10c8061dab87e2b12429c455fe4113e62a69f8e0/toss-web/server/data/modules.ts#L510-L514).

For **stock optimization**, the AI can identify dead stock (items not sold in X days) and prompt actions: _"You have 15 items not sold in 100 days - consider marking them clearance."_ It can also highlight potential theft or shrinkage issues: _"Your last 3 stock counts showed consistent losses of similar items (like 2-3 units of brand Y missing each time) - possible pattern of shrinkage."_ That might lead Maria to improve security or check staff handling.

AI also assists in **valuation and cost**: For instance, if costs of an item are rising, it could warn to adjust pricing or negotiate with supplier. It might say _"The last purchase price for Item Z increased by 15%. This will reduce your margin if selling at old price."_ so Maria can react by raising prices or finding alternatives.

Another clever insight is **spoilage prediction** (for perishable goods). If some items have expiry dates recorded, AI can look at sales velocity and tell you if you're on track to sell them before expiry or not. E.g., _"Milk Batch 20230401 will expire in 10 days, and you still have 20 units. At current daily sales (2/day) you will have ~0-5 unsold by expiry. Consider a discount to boost sales_[_\[37\]_](https://github.com/Toss-Online-Services/TossErp/blob/10c8061dab87e2b12429c455fe4113e62a69f8e0/toss-web/server/data/modules.ts#L510-L514)_."_

For multi-location businesses or the network, AI can suggest **stock redistribution**: _"Warehouse A has excess of Item P (100 units, 3 months of stock) while Warehouse B is low (2 days of stock). Suggest transferring 50 units from A to B."_

**Collaborative Network Features:** Inventory collaboration in the network layer opens up possibilities like **stock sharing** and **group inventory visibility**. For example, network members can opt into a system where they can see if a nearby shop has an item in stock that they ran out of. This is facilitated through TOSS by each shop's Stock module sharing some data (perhaps just item availability and quantity) in a communal lookup. So if a customer comes to Shop A and an item is out, the owner can quickly check the network stock and say "Try Shop B on Main Street, they have it." This improves customer service and keeps the sale within the network (the customer might have otherwise gone to a big supermarket).

Taking it further, the network could establish **stock pools** for certain goods. For instance, a group of shops might collectively maintain a central buffer stock of certain fast-moving items. TOSS would represent this as a central warehouse (belonging to the network or a lead member) that each shop can draw from. The Stock module's transfer capabilities then handle requests: when Shop A is low, they create a stock transfer request from Central to A. This could even be automated by AI triggers (like a central inventory system that sees Shop A hit threshold and prompts a replenishment from central). Such pooling helps smaller shops handle spikes in demand without individually holding huge safety stocks - the network buffer absorbs the variability.

Another collaborative aspect is **joint inventory financing**: sometimes networks get inventory on consignment or credit from a supplier as a group. The Stock module can mark those items such that when they sell at each shop, it records liability back to the supplier. For example, a supplier might stock 100 units across 5 shops (20 each) but only charge for what sells. TOSS can track this by item and location, and a network coordinator can see in aggregate how much has sold and needs to be paid for. This is complex but doable - effectively multi-tenant inventory with a shared ownership model.

Moreover, the network can perform **collective inventory analysis**: see which products are most popular across the entire network, and share that info with members or use it to negotiate better wholesale deals. The Stock module data from all members can feed into a central dashboard (without exposing individual sensitive info like exact stock levels per shop unless needed). For example, _"Network X sold 5000 loaves of bread this week in total_[_\[86\]_](https://docs.frappe.io/erpnext/v12/user/manual/en/stock#:~:text=,Provide%20valuation%20of%20each%20product)_. It seems bread demand is rising 10% week-over-week consistently."_ This might prompt the network to coordinate with bakeries for assured supply or volume discounts.

Finally, in terms of **disaster recovery**, if one member faces a shortage or emergency (say a storage issue ruined their stock), the network inventory visibility and solidarity allows others to pitch in. They could rapidly transfer stock to that member to keep them running. TOSS would handle those inter-company transfers and any financial settlements (like selling at cost to the peer).

In essence, the Stock module at the network level transforms isolated silos of inventory into a more flexible, interlinked supply web. Businesses benefit from each other's foresight and safety stocks, reduce lost sales by directing customers within the network, and strengthen their negotiation and resilience by acting as one large inventory system with distributed nodes rather than many tiny ones.

## Accounting (Finance Module)

**Functional Overview & Purpose:** The Accounting module handles the financial backbone of the platform, recording all **financial transactions** and providing clarity on the business's financial health. It covers bookkeeping tasks such as invoicing, payments, expenses, and financial reporting. For small businesses in townships, TOSS simplifies accounting into an understandable "money in, money out, what's left" format while still maintaining rigorous double-entry records in the background[\[38\]](https://github.com/Toss-Online-Services/TossErp/blob/10c8061dab87e2b12429c455fe4113e62a69f8e0/.cursor/rules/toss.mdc#L202-L208). The goal is to give business owners insight into profitability and cash flow without requiring them to be accounting experts.

**Core Workflows:**

- **Sales & Receivables:** Whenever a **Sales Invoice** is created in Selling (including POS invoices), it records revenue and an amount receivable from the customer[\[39\]](https://docs.frappe.io/erpnext/v13/user/manual/en/accounts#:~:text=In%20ERPNext%2C%20accounting%20operations%20consists,of%203%20main%20transactions). If the sale is paid immediately (cash sale), the receivable is immediately settled (the system can auto-create a Payment entry or mark invoice as paid). Outstanding invoices remain in Accounts Receivable. The owner can monitor who hasn't paid and for how long. Sending payment reminders or statements can be done for overdue accounts.
- **Purchases & Payables:** Similarly, each **Purchase Invoice** (supplier bill) recorded in Buying logs an expense (or asset stock increase) and an amount payable to the supplier[\[89\]](https://docs.frappe.io/erpnext/v13/user/manual/en/accounts#:~:text=,payments%2C%20credit%20and%20other%20types). Unpaid bills sit in Accounts Payable. When the business pays a supplier, a Payment entry is made which reduces that liability.
- **Expense Recording:** Not all expenses come with formal purchase invoices. For small cash expenses (transport, tea for staff, etc.), the owner can record a **Payment (Expense)** directly: choose an expense account (like "Transport Expense"), amount, and that immediately reflects in accounts (credit Cash, debit Transport Expense). TOSS provides a simple form for such outflows, essentially a Payment Entry not tied to a supplier invoice.
- **Cash Management:** The module helps manage cash on hand and bank balances. Daily cash sales can be reconciled through a **Cash Register** or **POS Closing** entry that the owner approves (as described in POS). If using multiple modes, the system tracks each (cash, mobile money, etc.). Bank deposits or withdrawals can be recorded as internal transfers between cash and bank accounts in TOSS, so that the books know where the money physically is.
- **Payroll & Other Journals:** If the business uses HR module, monthly **Payroll Journal Entries** can be posted (salary expenses, liabilities for deductions). Also, any other adjustments (like depreciation from the Assets module, or owner's drawings, etc.) reflect via **Journal Entries**. TOSS hides complexity by having dedicated forms: e.g., a Depreciation form in Assets posts a journal automatically. But an accountant user can manually create a journal for any misc. entry (like correcting an error, etc.).
- **Financial Reporting:** The module produces standard reports - **Income Statement** (Profit & Loss), **Balance Sheet**, and **Cash Flow Statement** - but in user-friendly terms. For example, an Income Statement might be labeled "Money In/Out Summary" showing total income, total expenses, and net profit ("What's left")[\[38\]](https://github.com/Toss-Online-Services/TossErp/blob/10c8061dab87e2b12429c455fe4113e62a69f8e0/.cursor/rules/toss.mdc#L202-L208). Balance Sheet might be simplified into "What you have (Assets) and What you owe (Liabilities)". There are also **General Ledger** and **Trial Balance** reports for accountants, and possibly simplified charts for the owner (like monthly profit trend, expense breakdown pie).
- **Taxation:** If the business is VAT-registered or subject to other taxes, the module will calculate tax on sales and purchases (from invoice entries) and can generate tax reports (e.g., VAT Return). In South Africa, for instance, it could help prepare the VAT201 form values. If not registered, tax settings can be turned off for simplicity.

**Business Rules & Validation:**

- **Double Entry Enforcement:** Every financial transaction must balance debits and credits. TOSS automates this behind scenes (e.g., when you record a sale for R100, it debits customer or cash and credits sales income R100[\[89\]](https://docs.frappe.io/erpnext/v13/user/manual/en/accounts#:~:text=,payments%2C%20credit%20and%20other%20types)). It will not allow any posting that doesn't balance. Journal entries require equal total debits and credits.
- **Fiscal Year and Period Lock:** The system can define fiscal year periods. Once accounts for a period are finalized, you can lock it (or at least require a higher role to post in closed periods). This prevents accidental late changes that mess up reports. Often a small biz might not need this, but it's available as they grow.
- **Chart of Accounts Simplicity:** TOSS comes with a pre-set Chart of Accounts suited to small retail/service: e.g., a **Cash** account, **Bank** account, **Inventory** asset, **Accounts Receivable**, **Accounts Payable**, a few common **Expense** accounts (like Purchases, Rent, Transport, Wages), and **Sales** income accounts[\[39\]](https://docs.frappe.io/erpnext/v13/user/manual/en/accounts#:~:text=In%20ERPNext%2C%20accounting%20operations%20consists,of%203%20main%20transactions). This is given by default. The business can add more if needed, but we avoid overwhelming them with too many categories initially. The system ensures transactions use valid accounts and segments.
- **Real-time Stock Integration:** If using perpetual inventory (which TOSS does by default to simplify things), any stock transaction posts to accounting immediately. For example, selling an inventory item not only credits sales and debits cash/receivable, but also credits inventory asset and debits cost of goods sold (COGS). This is done under the hood. Thus, the books always reflect actual stock value. The rule is you can't directly post to stock accounts via a journal - you must use stock module flows, preserving the subledger integrity.
- **Payment Matching:** A payment entry must be correctly linked to an invoice (or marked as advance). TOSS helps by providing a list of unpaid invoices to allocate payment against. It prevents common errors like applying a payment to a wrong customer's invoice.
- **Currency and Exchange:** If the business deals in multiple currencies (less likely for local small business, but possible e.g., dealing with USD wholesale or neighboring country currency), the system enforces that each invoice/payment has an explicit currency and rate. Financial reports can be in base currency with gains/losses calculated on exchange rate differences when payments settle.
- **Tax Calculations:** If taxes apply, all sales and purchase forms must have a tax template selected. The system will compute tax and keep it in a tax liability account. It validates that values are within logical range (e.g., not more tax than total, etc.). Also, if an item is tax-exempt, it ensures that's respected per item setting.
- **Owner's Equity Tracking:** If the owner injects personal money or withdraws cash for personal use, these should be recorded via journal entries (credit Owner Contribution or debit Drawings). TOSS might present a simplified interface "Record Money Taken by Owner" which behind scenes posts the journal. This ensures the business accounts clearly separate personal and business funds (important for clarity and possible future financing).
- **Audit Trail:** The Accounting module keeps an audit log of edits and deletions of transactions. E.g., if an invoice is cancelled or edited, it is logged. This maintains the trustworthiness of records, which is crucial if statements will be shown to lenders or partners.

**Required Data Models & Key Fields:**

- **Chart of Accounts:** A hierarchical structure of accounts. Each Account has fields: Account Name, Account Type (Asset/Liability/Equity/Income/Expense), possibly a parent (for grouping, e.g., all expense accounts under Expenses), and Currency if it's a foreign currency account. The COA defines where each transaction goes.
- **Journal Entry:** Generic accounting entry. Fields - Posting Date, Reference (could be a memo), and a table of **Journal Items** (Account, Debit amount, Credit amount, and optional dimensions like which customer or cost center). Most entries (invoices, payments) won't be made as manual journals, but all eventually create journal entries in the backend ledger.
- **Sales Invoice:** (from Selling) Fields of note for accounting - whether it's paid or not, posting date, due date, and it generates receivable line items. It has an "Is POS" or "Is Paid" flag to indicate if cash.
- **Purchase Invoice:** Fields - posting date, due date, bill date/number, etc., and generates payable entries.
- **Payment Entry:** Fields - Party Type (Customer/Supplier), Party (name), Payment Type (Receive or Pay), Mode of Payment (Cash/Bank/Mobile), Amount, and references to which invoices are being paid (with allocation amounts). Also has fields for source/destination accounts (e.g., Debiting bank and crediting receivables for a customer payment).
- **Expense Claim/Petty Cash Voucher:** If present (from HR or stand-alone), an employee expense claim might lead to accounting entries (but in a micro setting, likely the owner just records directly as expense or uses Payment Entry).
- **GL Entry:** The fundamental log of each debit/credit posted. Fields - Account, Date, Amount, Debit/Credit flag, Voucher Type (e.g., Sales Invoice), Voucher No, Party (if relevant). These entries are what the reports aggregate. TOSS generates these from other docs.
- **Company:** (Master data, holds base currency, default fiscal year, etc. - relevant for multi-tenant, each business is a Company in ERPNext terms).
- **Fiscal Year:** Defines year start/end, maybe periods. Used to filter reports, etc.
- **Cost Centers:** Possibly used to track expenses by project or department if needed (more advanced, likely one cost center if at all in small biz).
- **Accounting Dimensions:** Could be used if network had cross-company projects etc., but likely not needed for initial scope.

**User Interface & Experience:** The Accounting module UI in TOSS is designed to present essential info in a digestible way. The **Financial Dashboard** might show: _Cash on Hand: R5,000; Bank Balance: R12,000; Unpaid Customer Invoices: R8,000; Unpaid Supplier Bills: R6,000; This Month's Profit: R3,000_ - using big numbers and maybe simple charts. It's essentially giving the owner the "money in, money out, what's left" view[\[38\]](https://github.com/Toss-Online-Services/TossErp/blob/10c8061dab87e2b12429c455fe4113e62a69f8e0/.cursor/rules/toss.mdc#L202-L208).

For someone without accounting knowledge, TOSS labels things simply. The Accounts Receivable list might be titled **"Customers Who Owe You Money"** and Accounts Payable **"Bills You Need to Pay"**[\[18\]](https://github.com/Toss-Online-Services/TossErp/blob/10c8061dab87e2b12429c455fe4113e62a69f8e0/.cursor/rules/toss.mdc#L294-L301). Each entry shows how much and since when. The owner can click an entry to drill down (e.g., see the invoice details, and from there perhaps send a reminder or record a payment).

Recording transactions is action-oriented: **"Record an Expense"** leads to a straightforward form: amount, what it was for (choose Expense category), date, and how you paid (cash/bank). Similarly, **"Record a Sale Payment"** (for when a customer pays later) asks: which customer and invoice, how much, payment mode. The system takes care of the accounting behind.

The Chart of Accounts is mostly behind the scenes for ordinary users. But if accessed, it might be presented in plain language - e.g., under Assets: "Cash in Shop", "Bank Account - FNB", "Stock on Hand"; under Liabilities: "Supplier Bills Payable", etc. The interface encourages not messing with it unless necessary.

The **Reports** are designed to be understandable: e.g., the Profit & Loss might literally say:

- **Money In (Income):** R50,000
- **Money Out (Expenses):** R44,000
- **What's Left (Profit):** R6,000 [\[38\]](https://github.com/Toss-Online-Services/TossErp/blob/10c8061dab87e2b12429c455fe4113e62a69f8e0/.cursor/rules/toss.mdc#L202-L208)

Then allow expanding to see categories (Income broken by Sales types, Expenses broken by salaries, rent, etc.). A Balance Sheet might say:

- Assets: Cash R5k + Stock R20k + Money owed by customers R8k = **Total Assets R33k**
- Liabilities: Loans R5k + Money owed to suppliers R4k = **Total Liabilities R9k**
- **Net Worth (Equity): R24k** (which came from initial owner's investment plus accumulated profit).

This framing helps non-accountants grasp their finances. Of course, standard terminology can be shown for those comfortable.

Another UI aspect is **automation**: many accounting entries auto-post from other modules. The owner rarely has to manually create a Journal. If they do (say, to record owner taking cash), TOSS might have a guided form: _"Owner drew R500 cash for personal use"_ and it will post debit Owner Drawings, credit Cash - without the user even seeing those account names explicitly. This reduces errors significantly.

**Offline Capabilities:** Core accounting entries are usually triggered by other activities (sales, purchases) which we've covered offline handling for. The Accounting module itself for analysis and reporting likely requires data to be synced to be meaningful. However, if the owner is offline, they can still input expenses or payments on their device - these will sit as pending transactions (much like offline invoices) and sync later. The impact on balances won't reflect for others until sync.

One should be cautious: two users offline could theoretically both pay the same invoice - but in a small biz, usually one person handles accounts. And TOSS on sync would catch that the invoice was already paid and prevent duplicate application.

So practically: if Maria pays a supplier while at a rural market with no signal, she can log that payment on her phone. When back online, it will mark the supplier bill as paid in the system for everyone (like her business partner) to see. The only thing she might not have offline is real-time bank balance if others also spent - but that's minor for short offline periods.

**Example Use Case (Township Context):** _Let's follow Naledi from the earlier examples, who runs the hardware store. At the end of the month, she wants to see how her business did. She opens the_ _Profit & Loss_ _report in TOSS. It shows:_ _Income: R80,000,_ _Expenses: R70,000,_ _Profit: R10,000_ _for the month of March. She expands Expenses: it lists major categories - e.g.,_ _Stock Purchases: R50,000,_ _Rent: R5,000,_ _Transport: R2,000,_ _Wages: R10,000,_ _Misc: R3,000_\*. This breakdown lets her see where money is going. She's a bit concerned that transport cost is high relative to sales. The AI in TOSS might even highlight: "Transport expense is 2.5% of revenue, which is above industry norm 1%" - prompting her to investigate if trips to suppliers can be optimized.

Naledi then checks her **Balance Sheet (What You Have vs Owe)**. It shows she has **Cash R5k**, **Bank R20k**, **Inventory R30k**, **Customers Owe (Receivables) R10k**; and on Liabilities: **Suppliers Owed R8k**, **Loan from Cooperative R15k**. So, assets total R65k, liabilities R23k, net worth ~R42k (which matches her initial investment plus profits retained). This gives Naledi a sense of security - she is in net positive territory.

She uses **Accounts Receivable** view to see who owes her. There are 3 customers with unpaid invoices: one hardware contractor owes R6k not yet due (30 days terms), one individual owes R3k overdue by 5 days, etc. She decides to send gentle reminders. She selects those invoices and hits **"Send Reminder"** - TOSS generates a polite email or SMS with the invoice details. (Because her business is small, she might also just call them - TOSS could log "Reminder call made" on those accounts.)

On the payables side, she sees **Bills to Pay**: Supplier A R4k (due in 10 days), Supplier B R4k (due next month). She plans her cash - she might pay Supplier A early to maintain goodwill, so she goes to **Payments** and records a **Payment Entry**: selects Supplier A, the invoice, and chooses Bank account (since she will do an EFT). TOSS then marks that bill as paid and reduces her Bank balance accordingly in the system.

Naledi also had taken a small loan of R20k from the local cooperative a year ago (recorded as a Loan liability). Each month she's paying R1k. TOSS has an **automated journal** (or a template) for this monthly entry: it credits Bank R1k and debits the Loan liability (and a bit to interest expense). She doesn't fully grok debits/credits, but she set up a **Repeating Entry** with help from an accountant. Now every month, it auto-posts on the due date[\[52\]](https://9t9it.com/eprnext-faq#:~:text=What%20features%20are%20available%20in,the%20HR%20module%20of%20ERPNext). She just needs to actually send the money via bank, which she does.

Because TOSS integrates inventory, when Naledi checked her P&L, the **Cost of Goods Sold** part (stock purchases consumption) was accurate without manual calculation - every item sold had automatically expensed its cost. In the past, she might have forgotten how much stock was used and misestimated profit. Now it's precise.

During an audit from a potential microfinance investor, Naledi can confidently produce her financial statements. The investor is impressed: everything is organized and there is an audit trail. For instance, they spot a big expense entry "Wages: R10k". With Naledi's permission, TOSS drills down to show that's composed of two Salary Journal entries of R5k each for two employees - records generated from HR module. All numbers tie out.

Naledi's trust in the Accounting module means she no longer fears "the books." It's all updated through her daily operations, and she can focus on decisions - like "Can I afford another assistant?" TOSS's cash flow projection might show, yes, with current trends she maintains a positive bank balance even after adding a R3k/month wage. Thus, Accounting in TOSS not only keeps her compliant and organized, it actively guides her financial strategy.\*

**SaaS 2.0 - AI & Insights:** The AI in the Accounting module acts as a financial advisor looking out for the business's well-being. One crucial insight it provides is **cash flow forecasting**. By analyzing outgoing payables and incoming receivables (and patterns of expenses), the AI projects the cash balance weeks ahead and can warn of shortfalls. For example, _"Alert: In 2 weeks you have large payments (supplier bills and rent) totaling R20k, but based on current cash and expected sales, you may be R5k short_[_\[28\]_](https://github.com/Toss-Online-Services/TossErp/blob/10c8061dab87e2b12429c455fe4113e62a69f8e0/.cursor/rules/toss.mdc#L114-L117)_. Consider ways to increase cash (like collect receivables or reduce expenses)."_ This advance warning helps Naledi avoid a crisis like not making rent.

AI also looks at **expense anomalies**. If this month's electricity bill is way higher than usual, it flags it: _"Your utility expense is 50% higher than last month_[_\[90\]_](https://github.com/Toss-Online-Services/TossErp/blob/10c8061dab87e2b12429c455fe4113e62a69f8e0/toss-web/server/data/modules.ts#L375-L384)_."_ It might be due to a rate hike or equipment issue - either way, it draws her attention to investigate. Similarly, it watches for creeping costs (e.g., stationery or data costs slowly rising each month).

Another feature is **margin analysis**. AI will continuously calculate product or service margins from integrated data (sales price vs cost) and identify any problematic trends: _"The margin on Product X has fallen to 5%. Check if purchase costs increased or price needs updating."_ If integrated with sales and stock, it can pinpoint where money might be leaking.

For businesses with debt or loans, AI monitors **financial ratios** somewhat. It might note: _"Your inventory turnover is low (stock sitting ~90 days on average). That's cash tied up - consider a sale to liquidate old stock,"_ linking inventory management with finances. Or, _"Your accounts receivable days are high (averaging 60 days). Maybe tighten credit terms to improve cash flow."_ These are things an experienced accountant would advise; AI brings that advice to someone like Naledi automatically in simple language.

AI can also optimize **bill payments**. For instance, if she has some early payment discounts available, it could suggest: _"If you pay Supplier B 10 days early, you save R200 in discounts. Currently, you have enough cash, so it's beneficial."_ Conversely, if cash is tight, it might suggest prioritizing which bills to pay first (taking into account due dates and any supplier relations).

With respect to **tax compliance**, AI can ensure she's setting aside enough for VAT or income tax. It could say: _"Based on this quarter's sales, expected VAT liability is ~R8k_[_\[39\]_](https://docs.frappe.io/erpnext/v13/user/manual/en/accounts#:~:text=In%20ERPNext%2C%20accounting%20operations%20consists,of%203%20main%20transactions)_. Make sure you have that reserved."_ Similarly for any other statutory dues.

When the network aspect is considered, if Naledi's data (anonymized) is part of a benchmark, AI might tell her: _"Your profit margin is 12%, while the average among similar shops in the network is 15%. Check if your costs (like cost of goods or rent) are higher, or if your pricing can be improved."_ This gives her context of performance beyond her own books.

**Collaborative Network Features:** On a network level, Accounting could enable things like **group financial services**. For example, a network of businesses might collectively approach a lender or grant program. With members' consent, they could produce combined financial statements or at least key figures (total turnover of network, combined profit, etc.) to strengthen their case. TOSS could aggregate these safely - e.g., a report showing "Network X total quarterly revenue: R500k, average profit margin: 10%" which can impress a microfinance institution to extend credit lines to the group.

Another collaborative feature is handling **inter-company transactions** within the network. If businesses trade amongst themselves (one's output is another's input), TOSS can streamline those entries by linking their accounting. For example, Shop A buys bulk rice and sells part of it to Shop B. In Shop A's books it's a sale, in Shop B's books a purchase. If both use TOSS and agree to link, a single entry could post to both: credit Shop A's sales and debit Shop B's purchases simultaneously (via the network layer). This ensures books reconcile and saves time. Of course, it would only happen with trust and arrangements in the network.

The network could also have shared **accounting services**. Perhaps they hire a bookkeeper to oversee all members' books monthly. That bookkeeper (with permission) could have access to each tenant's Accounting module via a unified interface, rather than logging into each separately. TOSS could provide an accountant portal to manage multiple companies (this is more of a multi-company feature). The benefit is cost-sharing on expert help, and uniform quality of bookkeeping. The accountant could also generate a combined report for the network leadership showing aggregate financial health without revealing individual secrets (similar to above point).

Additionally, network-wide **financial training or policies** can be rolled out. For instance, they might set a norm that all members maintain at least 10% of monthly sales as cash reserve. TOSS can monitor and notify the network committee if someone is struggling to do so (could be a sign they need assistance). Or if the network has a group insurance or emergency fund, TOSS Accounting can facilitate contributions to it - automatically deducting a tiny percentage of each sale across members into a "Network Fund" account (in each member's books as an expense, and in a central network account as income).

Another scenario: a network might collectively guarantee a member's loan. If that member defaults, the network covers it. TOSS could track such cross-liability. If Member A misses a payment, it could flag to network treasurer, and if it's paid from a central fund, the system could record due from Member A to network. This is complex but demonstrates how integrated accounting can support mutual aid frameworks.

Lastly, transparency can be increased within safe limits. Some networks might choose to share certain financial metrics internally to encourage mutual improvement or trust. For example, they could agree to share revenue figures or expense ratios. TOSS could have a dashboard for members that shows anonymized quartiles or average with theirs. E.g., "Your monthly sales are in the top 25% of the network" or "Your rent-to-sales ratio is higher than 80% of members." This might drive collaborative initiatives like bulk rent negotiation or sharing of high-performing tactics.

In summary, while each business keeps its own books in TOSS, the network layer can pool financial knowledge and strength - enabling collective bargaining, support, and perhaps even shared fiscal responsibility. Accounting data, when aggregated and permissioned correctly, becomes a powerful tool for communal advancement and resilience in the township economy.

## Manufacturing (Production Module)

**Functional Overview & Purpose:** The Manufacturing module helps businesses plan and manage the process of converting raw materials into finished products. It covers everything from defining the **Bill of Materials (BOM)** for a product to scheduling production, issuing materials, and tracking work-in-progress and outputs[\[41\]](https://docs.frappe.io/erpnext/user/manual/en/manufacturing#:~:text=The%20Manufacturing%20module%20in%20ERPNext,conduct%20various%20manufacturing%20operations%20effectively)[\[45\]](https://docs.frappe.io/erpnext/user/manual/en/manufacturing#:~:text=,and%20optimizing%20production%20runs%20to). For small manufacturers like bakeries, furniture makers, or artisans, this module ensures that they can efficiently produce goods, control production costs, and meet customer demand on time. It tightly integrates with Stock (for raw material consumption and finished goods stocking), Buying (for procuring materials), and Accounting (for costing and work-in-progress value).

**Core Workflows:**
