- **Bill of Materials (BOM) Definition:** Create a BOM for each manufactured product, listing all the raw materials (and their quantities) and any sub-assemblies required to make one unit of finished product[\[45\]](https://docs.frappe.io/erpnext/user/manual/en/manufacturing#:~:text=,and%20optimizing%20production%20runs%20to). For example, a wooden table's BOM might include: 5 planks of wood, 20 screws, 100ml varnish. A BOM can also include labor or machine time for cost purposes (e.g., 2 hours carpentry labor).
- **Work Order (Production Order):** When it's time to produce, create a Work Order specifying what product to make, how many, and which BOM to use[\[45\]](https://docs.frappe.io/erpnext/user/manual/en/manufacturing#:~:text=,and%20optimizing%20production%20runs%20to). For instance, "Produce 10 Tables using Standard Table BOM." The Work Order serves as the central document to track that production batch. It can include target start and end dates.
- **Material Issue (for Production):** Before or during production, **Issue Raw Materials** to manufacturing. TOSS can automatically pull the exact quantities from BOM for the given production quantity. The storekeeper or production lead then confirms issuing those from stock (this creates a Stock Entry of type "Material Transfer" from raw material store to Work in Progress or consumes them directly). This ensures raw material inventory is decreased accordingly.
- **Production Execution:** If detailed tracking is needed, the module can break the Work Order into **Operations** (steps) performed at **Workstations** (e.g., Cutting, Assembly, Finishing)[\[45\]](https://docs.frappe.io/erpnext/user/manual/en/manufacturing#:~:text=,and%20optimizing%20production%20runs%20to). Operators can mark each operation complete, possibly using **Job Cards** for time tracking at each step. For simple cases, these steps can be skipped, and one can just mark the Work Order as started and then finished when done.
- **Quality Checks & Approvals:** (Integration with Quality module) During or after production, perform quality inspections (e.g., check dimensions, strength, or taste for food) as defined for the product. The Work Order might require a quality check sign-off before completion[\[43\]](https://docs.frappe.io/erpnext/user/manual/en/manufacturing#:~:text=,insights%20into%20production%20performance%2C%20costs).
- **Finish Goods Receipt:** Once production is completed (all operations done and quality passed), **Receive Finished Goods** into stock. In TOSS, this can be done by submitting a **Work Order Completion** which automatically creates a Stock Entry of type "Manufacture/Repack" - moving the raw materials out of WIP (or consuming them) and adding the finished product into Finished Goods stock. For example, after producing 10 tables, stock of "Table" item increases by 10, and the wood, screws, etc. are reduced accordingly.
- **Costing:** The module calculates the total production cost for the Work Order. It sums material costs (based on stock valuations issued) and can include operation costs (either from labor time logs or predetermined rates). This yields a cost per unit produced. TOSS updates the valuation of the finished goods accordingly in inventory (so the 10 tables carry the correct manufacturing cost). If there is a variance (like usage of more material than BOM or scrap), it's recorded as a **Manufacturing Variance** for analysis.
- **Production Planning (optional):** For more complex operations, one can use a **Production Plan** to consolidate demand (sales orders or stock refill levels) and generate multiple Work Orders and material procurement requirements at once. This might be more than a micro unit needs, but it's there if scaling up.

**Business Rules & Validation:**

- **BOM Accuracy:** A BOM must be **Approved** before use (to avoid using a draft by mistake). TOSS ensures BOMs don't have circular references (A BOM referring to itself via sub-assembly) and that quantities are sensible (no zero quantities). Only one BOM is usually marked default per item.
- **Work Order Quantity vs. BOM:** If you make a different quantity than BOM standard, TOSS scales the material requirements. E.g., BOM for 1 cake needs 2kg flour; a Work Order for 5 cakes will demand 10kg flour. It validates material availability: if you don't have enough on hand, it will warn or prevent starting the Work Order unless you allow it (which could result in negative stock for materials).
- **Material Consumption Enforcement:** The system expects you to issue the materials either up-front or during production. By completion, all required materials should be issued (or you explicitly mark some as not used or extra used). If a Work Order completion is attempted without issuing enough raw materials, TOSS warns you (or auto-issues from stock if configured to backflush).
- **Operation Completion:** If using operations and job cards, the system can enforce sequence (can't mark Assembly done before Cutting is done, etc.). It can also ensure that all operations are completed (or explicitly skipped) before allowing final completion of the Work Order.
- **Quality Hold:** Optionally, if quality tests fail, the Work Order cannot be completed (or the product goes into a "Rejected" stock location). This ensures faulty goods aren't counted as good stock.
- **Scrap Accounting:** If some raw material ends up as scrap (offcuts, wastage) or some finished products are defective, TOSS can capture those. Scrap can be defined on BOM (expected % waste) or entered at finish. The rule is that total material output (finished + scrap) should tally with input or else show as unexplained loss. This reflects in costing as well.
- **Auto-Accounting:** Upon completion, the module posts necessary accounting: credit raw material inventory, debit WIP (if tracked) or expenses, and debit finished goods inventory, credit WIP. If using a WIP account, Work Orders serve to collect costs. Simpler approach: TOSS might directly expense variances to a "Manufacturing variance" account and move materials to finished goods at standard cost. Either way, it ensures books reflect the production.
- **Concurrency and Capacity:** If desired, the system can check **Workstation capacity** (e.g., machine can only handle 5 units a day). But for simplicity, this might be turned off unless needed. Without capacity planning, overlapping work orders are allowed unless manually monitored.
- **Production Order Closure:** A Work Order can be closed if it's not going to be fully completed. For instance, you planned 100 units but decided to stop at 95 due to scrap. Closing it finalizes it with less output; TOSS might treat the 5 short as cancelled or as scrap. It should prompt what to do with remaining materials (return to stock or mark as waste).

**Required Data Models & Key Fields:**

- **BOM:** Fields - Item (finished good), Quantity (of finished good that this BOM yields, typically 1), BOM Items (table of raw materials: each row with Item Code, Required Qty per FG qty, maybe Scrap %), Operations (table if any: Operation name, time per unit or batch, Workstation, labor cost). Also a field for BOM cost (system calculated from items and operation rates).
- **Work Order:** Fields - Product to manufacture, Quantity to manufacture, BOM to use, Status (Not Started, In Process, Completed, Closed), Planned Start/End dates, Actual Start/End, and links to stock entries (material issues and completions) and any job cards. Also can have a Source like which Sales Order it's for.
- **Operation:** (Master for types of work) Fields - Operation name, description, Workstation Type or default workstation, and maybe standard time or cost.
- **Workstation:** Fields - Name, possibly capacity (units per hour or hours per day available), cost per hour (for cost calculations), and maintenance schedule if relevant.
- **Job Card:** If used - Fields: Work Order, Operation, Employee/Person who did it, Start Time, End Time, Quantity produced in that step (if incremental), status (Pending/Completed). This logs actual operation time and progress.
- **Stock Entry (for Manufacture):** A specific kind of stock entry with two sets of child tables - one for consumed items (materials) and one for produced items (finished goods and by-products, if any). This is usually auto-generated by Work Order completion in TOSS rather than manual.
- **Batch & Serial:** If finished goods or raw materials are batch/serial tracked, BOM can note if it inherits serial (like assembly keeps component serial numbers). Work Order completion will then require entering serial numbers for output (if serialized) and listing which serials of inputs were used.
- **Production Plan:** Fields - list of Sales Orders or items needed, and it will generate suggested Work Orders and Material Requests. (This is advanced usage; likely optional for most small cases.)

**User Interface & Experience:** The Manufacturing interface is streamlined for ease of use on a small scale. The **Work Order list** gives a quick view of each production job's status, e.g., "Table (10 units) - In Process - 80%". Clicking one opens a **Progress View**: at the top, basic info like item and quantity, below that maybe a visual timeline of operations or a checklist (if not using operations, it could simply show material status and a big "Complete Order" button when ready).

For those not familiar with manufacturing jargon, TOSS might label Work Orders as **"Production Job"** or **"Batch"**. On mobile, creating a new production job could be as simple as selecting the finished item and quantity - if only one BOM exists, it's auto-selected. Then you see a screen: "Materials Needed: 5x Wood Plank (50 in stock), 20x Screws (200 in stock), ... \[Issue All Materials\]". The **Issue All Materials** button creates the stock issue entry automatically. If stock is short, it would highlight in red or prompt to order missing parts (with a one-click to create a Purchase Order for shortages).

If operations are not heavily used, TOSS likely won't force the user through them - maybe just an optional tab "Operations" to mark if they want to track. For many, they'll skip straight to completion once materials are issued and time has passed. The **Complete Production** action would show: "You planned 10 units. Enter how many finished actually made: \[10\]. Any scrap or unused materials? \[maybe list items and allow adjust\]." After confirmation, it posts the stock entry and closes the Work Order.

For those tracking detail: each Operation in a Work Order can be shown as a card with "Start" and "Finish" buttons (or a field to enter time taken). If a worker uses the system, they might just see their assigned operation on an app and hit start when begin and finish when done; the Work Order updates accordingly.

The BOM editor UI is important: it should allow quick adding of items, maybe by searching the item name, entering quantity, and perhaps scrap %. Possibly upload from an existing template (copy another BOM). Since BOMs might be created rarely, it's okay if that's a bit more advanced.

TOSS can also have a **Production Analytics** view: e.g., how many of each product made this month, production efficiency (if times are tracked vs standard), and a cost summary. This is useful to see trends (like cost of wood rising reflected in higher table cost now than before).

**Offline Capabilities:** Production often happens in a workshop that might not have internet. So TOSS offline is handy for shop floor logging. Workers could receive a synced Work Order in the morning, then go offline. They can check off operations or log issues offline. If quality inspectors are walking around with a tablet, they can input results offline into the Work Order's quality section.

Issuing materials might require connection if one needs real-time stock check, but if pre-planned, one could also issue offline (it will sync and decrement stock when connected; risk of conflict is if someone else also moved that stock concurrently - but in a small setup usually one person handles it).

Completing a Work Order offline will generate the stock entry queued locally. Until sync, central inventory won't show the finished goods. But once connected, everything updates: raw stocks decrement, finished goods increment. If any conflict (like those materials were somehow consumed by something else in the interim), the sync will flag discrepancy to reconcile (rare scenario in practice with small teams).

**Example Use Case (Township Context):** \*Akhona runs a small bakery, making bread and pastries each morning. She uses TOSS Manufacturing to plan her daily baking. She has a BOM for "Bread Loaf" that lists: 500g flour, 300ml water, 10g yeast, a pinch of salt and sugar (negligible cost). For "Dozen Scones," a BOM with flour, butter, sugar, etc., is defined too.

Each evening, Akhona checks tomorrow's orders (from Sales) and shop stock. She decides to make 50 loaves and 20 dozens of scones tomorrow. In TOSS, she creates two **Work Orders**: one for 50 Bread Loaves, one for 20 Dozen Scones. The app immediately shows how much of each ingredient is needed. She sees she has enough of everything except yeast - yeast is a bit short for the bread. She clicks **"Shortfall -> Create Purchase Request"**. This raises a quick Material Request for yeast which she sends to her ingredient supplier via WhatsApp that night. The supplier delivers early morning and she records the Purchase Receipt, adding the yeast into stock.

In the morning, before mixing, she goes to the Bread Work Order on her tablet and hits **"Issue Materials"**. It subtracts the required flour, yeast, etc. from stock (she's weighed them out ready to mix). Similarly for scones. Now as she bakes, if she had help, she might use operations like "Mixing, Baking, Packaging" with times, but since it's just her and one assistant, she skips detailed logging. By 9am, all bread and scones are done.

She opens the Work Order in TOSS, taps **"Complete Production"**. For bread, it asks "Actual produced vs planned?". Overnight some bread burned, so she got 48 good loaves out of planned 50. She enters 48. It then asks what happened to the other 2 - she marks them as **Scrap** due to burn (so it will still account for the used ingredients). Upon submission, TOSS moves 48 Bread into Finished Goods stock (now available for sale)[\[43\]](https://docs.frappe.io/erpnext/user/manual/en/manufacturing#:~:text=,insights%20into%20production%20performance%2C%20costs), and the ingredients corresponding to 50 loaves are fully consumed (the cost of those 2 burnt loaves stays in a "Scrap Expense" account).

For scones, all 20 dozens came out fine, so she completes with 20 and no scrap. Those are added to stock, ingredients consumed.

Now throughout the day, her front-of-house sells the bread and scones via POS. Inventory goes down accordingly. At day's end, Akhona looks at the Production Analytics: it shows bread yield was 96% (48/50) and scones 100%. It also shows cost per loaf computed - she notices it's slightly higher today because yeast price went up. She decides it might be time to adjust her bread price up a little to maintain margin.

The Manufacturing module helped Akhona ensure she had the ingredients ready (flagging the yeast shortage) and accounted for everything that happened (even burnt bread). Over time, if she sees frequent scrap, she can adjust her BOMs or processes (or add a bit of scrap allowance in BOM so it doesn't always show as variance). It gives her a clear view of her production efficiency and cost, which is crucial for pricing and minimizing waste.\*

**SaaS 2.0 - AI & Insights:** AI in Manufacturing acts like a process engineer and production planner for Akhona. One of its roles is **demand forecasting for production**: based on historical sales and maybe external factors (weather, day of week), it can suggest how much to produce. For instance, _"Typically on Tuesdays you sell ~40 loaves. Tomorrow is a public holiday, demand might be higher - recommend baking 60 loaves."_ It could also look at current stock and say _"You still have 5 loaves unsold from today (stale risk), so maybe reduce tomorrow's production by that much."_ This helps optimize production quantity to meet demand without excessive leftovers.

AI can also optimize **BOM and processes** by analyzing usage vs output. If consistently, say, bread yields are 96%, AI might learn that 4% scrap is normal and adjust expectations (or suggest tweaks: _"You often burn ~4% of loaves - consider lowering oven temp or bake time slightly as an experiment."_). If an ingredient's actual usage often differs from BOM (maybe recipes are adjusted on the fly), AI will flag that and suggest updating the BOM to reality or investigate why more/less is used.

In terms of operations, if Akhona had multiple staff or machines, AI could do **production scheduling**: deciding which products to make first or how to sequence to maximize efficiency (e.g., _"Bake breads before pastries because oven is already hot from bread, saving energy."_ or _"Mix all doughs in one go if capacity allows, then bake - to reduce idle time."_). In larger settings, it might allocate work to different workstations to balance load.

For **maintenance** (though part of Quality or Assets), AI might watch production metrics to predict if equipment could be failing - e.g., _"Oven took longer to bake this week (noted by extended operation times), possible element issue - check before it breaks."_

AI also contributes to **cost control**: it monitors the cost of each batch and identifies trends. _"The cost per loaf has increased 10% due to ingredient price rises_[_\[30\]_](https://github.com/Toss-Online-Services/TossErp/blob/10c8061dab87e2b12429c455fe4113e62a69f8e0/toss-web/server/data/modules.ts#L274-L282)_. Suggest either raising price or finding cheaper supplier for flour."_ Or _"Labor time per batch is increasing, perhaps due to inexperienced staff - consider refresher training to improve speed."_

For multi-product environments, AI can advise on **product mix**: e.g., _"Bread has a higher profit margin and demand than scones - allocate more flour to bread production if resources are constrained."_

**Collaborative Network Features:** In a network context, Manufacturing collaboration might involve **shared production facilities** or **outsourced work among members**. For example, if one bakery in the network has excess oven capacity and another's oven broke, the first could temporarily bake for the second. Through TOSS, a Work Order could be assigned to a different business's facility. In practice, one company could issue a **Subcontract Work Order** to another: TOSS would create the necessary accounting (selling of service, etc.)[\[48\]](https://github.com/Toss-Online-Services/TossErp/blob/10c8061dab87e2b12429c455fe4113e62a69f8e0/toss-web/server/data/modules.ts#L430-L438). The network could coordinate such resource sharing, with TOSS tracking who provided materials and labor and how the final product is transferred.

The network could also standardize BOMs for similar products across members, sharing best recipes and methods. For instance, a co-op of soap makers might unify a base formula. TOSS can distribute a BOM template through the network so each member's Manufacturing module uses the proven recipe (they can adjust for local tweaks, but a baseline is shared).

Additionally, networks can aggregate **raw material procurement for production**. Through Buying collab, members already bulk-buy. The Manufacturing modules of each could be fed by a central material warehouse. It's similar to stock pooling: raw materials could be stored centrally and issued to members' Work Orders on demand, with TOSS handling the inter-company stock issues. This ensures each small producer always has materials when needed without individually storing large stocks.

On a more strategic level, the network might decide to specialize production among members: e.g., Bakery A focuses on bread, B on pastries, then they swap products to sell a full range. Their Manufacturing schedules could be coordinated via network planning. TOSS could support by letting one member's Sales Orders generate another member's Work Orders automatically. Say Bakery B gets a big cake order but is at capacity; the network portal shows this to other bakers who can take the job. If Bakery C accepts, a Work Order appears in C's system to make the cake, and upon completion, the cake is transferred to B for delivery. All the monetary settlement can be handled through linked accounting entries (B paying C for subcontracting). The **Subcontracting** feature in ERPNext is analogous to this scenario[\[48\]](https://github.com/Toss-Online-Services/TossErp/blob/10c8061dab87e2b12429c455fe4113e62a69f8e0/toss-web/server/data/modules.ts#L430-L438).

Quality and training are also collaborative: network can implement a unified **Quality Management System** for all producers. TOSS Quality data from each (like failure rates, inspections) can be reviewed collectively to improve processes. If one member has a particularly efficient process (yield, time), others can learn from them through the network's knowledge sharing.

Finally, for marketing, the network might label products collectively (like a brand for a co-op). Ensuring consistency via Manufacturing module is key. The network might periodically audit each member's production (with TOSS checklists) to maintain quality standards for the common brand. Those audits (like Good Manufacturing Practice checks) can be scheduled and recorded in each Manufacturing/Quality module but overseen by the network committee.

In summary, the Manufacturing module within a network enables a distributed manufacturing system where capacity, knowledge, and resources are shared. Small local manufacturers can work together almost like departments of a larger factory - coordinating production, helping each other, and collectively meeting larger demands than any could alone, all facilitated by the integrated information flows in TOSS.

## Projects (Project Management Module)

**Functional Overview & Purpose:** The Projects module assists in planning and tracking project-based work - any endeavor with multiple tasks, a timeline, and deliverables. This could be external (e.g., a client project or a community construction job) or internal (setting up a new shop, a marketing campaign). It provides tools to break work into **tasks**, assign responsibilities, track deadlines, and monitor progress and costs. For small businesses, formal project management might seem heavy, but many can benefit from it - like a plumber scheduling a set of repairs, or a tailoring shop managing a large order with various steps. The Projects module ensures such activities are executed efficiently and that nothing falls through the cracks.

**Core Workflows:**

- **Project Creation:** Define a **Project** when there's a significant piece of work involving multiple steps or people. Give it a name, description, start and end date, and possibly associate it with a customer (if it's for a client) or with an internal goal. For example, "Community Center Paint Job - Client: ABC Org - Start May 1, End May 7."
- **Task Breakdown:** Under the project, list out **Tasks** that need to be completed[\[49\]](https://discuss.frappe.io/c/erpnext/projects/53#:~:text=Welcome%20to%20the%20ERPNext%20Proejcts,discuss%2C%20and%20help%20other%20communi%E2%80%A6). Each task is a specific piece of work with its own due date and assignee. For the painting job, tasks might be "Buy paint and materials", "Prep walls (cleaning and sanding)", "Apply primer coat", "Paint walls color coat", "Clean up site".
- **Assignment & Scheduling:** Assign each task to a person (or team). Set deadlines for each task (the system can also auto-stagger if you input task durations and dependencies). If certain tasks must happen in sequence, set dependencies (e.g., "Paint color coat" depends on finishing "Primer coat"). The module can then show a simple Gantt chart or timeline of the tasks.
- **Execution & Updates:** Team members can view their tasks (say via a to-do list or calendar). As they work, they can update tasks - mark them as In Progress or Completed, add notes or attach photos (e.g., a photo of the completed paint job). The project manager (or business owner) can monitor progress through a Project dashboard that might show percent complete or highlight any task overdue.
- **Time & Cost Tracking:** If needed, record **Time Logs** or **Expense Logs** against tasks. For instance, an electrician logs 3 hours on the "Wiring" task of a project, or a volunteer logs hours on a community project (which might not bill to a client but is tracked for accountability). Expenses like bought materials can also link to the project so you know total project cost.
- **Milestones & Billing:** Projects can have **Milestones** (key events/deliverables). In client projects, milestones often tie to payments. TOSS allows linking a Sales Invoice to a project or milestone. E.g., invoice 50% upon milestone "Primer done" and 50% on "Project completion." The module then shows what's been billed vs. what's left. For internal projects, this can be skipped.
- **Project Completion & Review:** Once all tasks are done (or the goal is reached), mark the project Completed/Closed. You can then review actual finish date vs planned, actual cost vs budget (if you set a budget), etc., to learn for next time.

**Business Rules & Validation:**

- **Task Dependencies:** If Task B depends on A, the system should not allow marking B complete until A is done, or at least warn if trying to start B early. Also, moving A's date should auto-adjust B's if possible (or flag a schedule slip).
- **Deadlines & Overdue:** The system automatically flags tasks that pass their due date without completion. These should be visible in red in the project view and in the assignee's task list. Optionally, send a reminder email/notification on the due date if not completed.
- **Resource Assignments:** Each task usually has one assignee. If multiple people, you might break it into sub-tasks or simply list multiple names in description. TOSS doesn't enforce workloads or hours by default, but if a user has too many tasks due same day, it's visible for the manager to reassign if needed. (There is capability for scheduling by capacity, but that may be overkill for now.)
- **Project Budget & Cost:** If you set a project budget (in terms of money or total hours), the system should track actuals and warn when nearing or exceeding budget. For example, if a project budget is R5k expenses and you log purchase receipts of R4.5k already, it might warn 90% budget used.
- **Linkage to Billing:** If using project-based billing, TOSS can ensure you don't miss invoicing for billable work. For instance, time logs can be marked billable, and you can generate an invoice from them. The rule might be that all billable hours should be invoiced when project completes or monthly, etc. The system can list unbilled costs for manager to invoice.
- **Access Control:** Possibly restrict who can see what projects. If you have external collaborators using the system (less likely, but suppose a client or subcontractor logs in to update tasks), you'd ensure they only see their project. But likely, one company uses it internally only.
- **Closure Conditions:** You shouldn't close a project if there are tasks not done (unless you explicitly cancel them). TOSS likely prompts to complete or delete remaining tasks. Also, if some budget left or invoices not done, it might remind the user.

**Required Data Models & Key Fields:**

- **Project:** Fields - Project Name, Customer (if applicable), Start Date, End Date (planned vs actual), Status (Open/Completed/Cancelled), Percent Complete (calculated from tasks done), Project Manager (could be the user responsible). Perhaps budget fields (planned cost, planned revenue).
- **Task:** Fields - Subject/Title, Description, Project (link), Assigned To (user), Status (Not Started, In Progress, Completed, etc.), Start Date, Due Date, Actual Completion Date, Priority (Low/Med/High), Depends On (link to another task). Could also have a field for estimated hours and actual hours logged.
- **Time Log:** If using - Fields: Project, Task (optional link), User, Date, Hours, Work Details description, Billable (yes/no), Billing Rate (if applicable).
- **Expense Log (or use Expense Claim/Purchase Invoice):** Simpler might be just tag any Purchase Invoice or expense Payment with a Project, so project cost is accumulated. If separate: Fields - Project, description, amount, date, maybe category of expense.
- **Milestone:** Could be a child table in Project with fields: Milestone name, target date, status (achieved/pending). If integrated with billing: milestone could have a billing percentage or amount.
- **Project Template:** For repeating types of projects, a template of tasks. Fields - name, and a set of tasks with relative dates (e.g., Day 1: X, Day 3: Y, etc.). Not critical but useful for standard projects like "New shop setup."
- **Issue:** (From support maybe, but if an issue/bug is linked to a project, might be tracked as well. Probably not in MVP need.)

**User Interface & Experience:** Managing a project in TOSS would typically involve a **Project Dashboard** view. When you open a project, you see the project's vital info at top (name, client, due date) and maybe a progress bar (e.g., "5 of 10 tasks completed - 50%"). Below that, tasks are often listed either in a hierarchical list or a board. In ERPNext, tasks can be indented or shown in Gantt. For mobile, likely a simple list sorted by due date with perhaps overdue tasks highlighted red, upcoming in green, etc.

Each **Task** entry shows a title, assignee, and maybe a checkbox for completion. Tapping it opens details where you can update status or reassign or change dates. Possibly an assignee can just swipe to mark complete or something.

There might be a toggle to view tasks grouped by status, or a **Kanban** board with columns To-Do, Doing, Done, which is intuitive and mobile-friendly (you could drag tasks between columns or use a menu to change status).

The **Gantt chart** view is useful on a larger screen - showing tasks on a timeline, dependencies lines. On mobile maybe not needed, but on the web interface that could be present. This helps visualize if the project is on schedule.

For team members, a **My Tasks** view aggregates tasks from all projects assigned to that user, sorted by due date. This is essentially their to-do list. They might just interact with that most of the time.

Logging time or expenses can be integrated: e.g., on a task, a user might click "Log Hours" and enter "2 hours - finished painting one wall" which then attaches to the task (and project) and updates progress possibly. Or they mark task done and then it might ask "how many hours did it take?" as a prompt if needed.

If using deadlines heavily, the system could send notifications: e.g., day before a task due, to the assignee. Or if a project is due next week and only 50% tasks done, a warning to the manager.

**Offline Capabilities:** Team members working on-site (like the painting crew at the community center) may have no net. TOSS offline allows them to open the project tasks list that was synced prior. They can check tasks off or add comments offline (like "need more paint, will resume tomorrow"). When back online, those updates sync, so the project manager sees them. If multiple people update offline, there's slight risk of conflicting edits (e.g., two people marking the same task complete - which is benign). TOSS can handle basic conflict by last update wins for status, or merging comments.

Creating new tasks offline also possible; they sync and appear for others. Say the team realized an extra task "fix crack in wall" - they add it offline, and once synced, the manager sees this new task added to scope.

The main limitation offline is no real-time collaboration - but team can work independently and sync later. For a short project, that's fine. Over a longer term, daily sync is enough.

**Example Use Case (Township Context):** _Jabulani has a small construction business. He got a contract to build a 2-room extension for a client. He sets this up as a_ _Project_ _in TOSS: "Ndaba House 2-Room Extension" with start today and deadline in 30 days, client Mr. Ndaba. He lists out_ _Tasks_\*: (1) Draw plan and get client approval, (2) Foundation and floor, (3) Walls up, (4) Roof installation, (5) Electrical wiring, (6) Plaster and finishing, (7) Painting, (8) Final cleanup and handover. He assigns himself to oversee, his bricklayer to tasks 2 and 3, a roofer sub-contractor to task 4 (notes that he's external), an electrician (task 5), and painter (task 7).

He sequences them with dependencies (foundation before walls, walls before roof, etc.). TOSS automatically spaces the Gantt chart roughly over the 30 days timeline, which he adjusts as needed (maybe he sets Walls to take 5 days after foundation done, etc.). The app highlights if any gap or overlap - he ensures there's not too much idle time but also that tasks that can parallel (e.g., electrical can do conduit while plastering maybe) are marked accordingly.

Throughout execution, Jabulani uses the **Project view** to monitor progress. When foundation is finished, he marks Task 2 complete (or his bricklayer does via the mobile app in the field). TOSS moves Task 3 (Walls) to "In Progress" automatically since dependency cleared. He also logs an **Expense** for cement and bricks used, linking it to the project (he simply does that by tagging the purchase invoice from the supplier with project name in TOSS). This way, under project costs he sees R15,000 spent on materials so far.

One week it rains heavily, causing delays. Jabulani goes into the project and shifts the Roof task 2 days later. TOSS warns this might push past deadline. Indeed, now Gantt shows finishing in 32 days. He informs the client and also tries to make up time later.

His electrician completes wiring and marks that task done via his phone. Immediately, Jabulani gets an app notification "Task 5 (Electrical wiring) completed by Samkelo." That triggers Jabulani to schedule an inspection of that work before plastering covers it. He adds a quick sub-task "Inspect wiring before plaster" and assigns himself, due next day. Offline or not, he notes after checking that all is good and marks that done.

At the end, the project finishes in 33 days (3 days late, mostly due to weather). Jabulani closes the project in TOSS. He reviews the **Project Report**: it shows 33 days actual vs 30 planned, and total cost R50k vs budget R45k (he sees that extra work like fixing a rain-damaged section and the delay cost some extra labor - he'll budget more contingency next time). He also sees total billed is R60k (he had invoiced the client in 3 stages from TOSS directly linked to project milestones). So profit was ~R10k on that project.

With this insight, Jabulani can refine quotes for future projects (maybe increase margin to cover unexpected delays). The Projects module helped him stay organized - he always knew what was done and what was left, even coordinating multiple trades (bricklayer, electrician, etc.). If the network or community asks for a report, he can produce a neat summary to show the work timeline and costs, lending professionalism to his small operation.\*

**SaaS 2.0 - AI & Insights:** For projects, AI becomes a **smart project assistant** that can predict pitfalls and optimize scheduling. For example, it can analyze Jabulani's past projects and say: _"Foundation tasks on average took 2 days longer than you planned, consider that in new schedules."_ Or _"Projects of this type (2-room extension) historically see 5% overspend on materials - perhaps add buffer in quote."_

During the project, AI can watch task progress and due dates to flag risks: _"Roof installation task is due in 2 days but its predecessor (Walls) is only 50% done - project likely to slip_[_\[51\]_](https://github.com/Toss-Online-Services/TossErp/blob/10c8061dab87e2b12429c455fe4113e62a69f8e0/toss-web/server/data/modules.ts#L360-L364)_."_ This early warning allows reallocation of resources or client notice. It could even suggest actions: _"Consider adding a second bricklayer to speed up Walls and meet the roof schedule."_

AI can also assist in **task prioritization**: if a user has many tasks across projects, it might generate a daily priority list: _"High priority today: complete Painting for Project X (deadline tomorrow), start Foundation for Project Y (to avoid rain forecast next week)."_ It's like having a project manager nudging the user.

For complex dependencies, the AI can run simulations (like PERT). For instance, _"There is a 80% chance project will finish by June 30; a 20% risk of running till July 5 due to known rainy season delays."_ This helps manage client expectations and push to mitigate (maybe building rain covers or adjusting order of tasks).

If multiple projects run in parallel, AI can alert if they will overload a particular resource. E.g., _"Your electrician is scheduled for full days on two projects next Thursday_[_\[50\]_](https://discuss.frappe.io/t/about-the-project-management-category/56267#:~:text=About%20the%20Project%20Management%20category,category%2C%20you%20can%20post)_. You need to reschedule tasks or get an additional electrician to avoid delay."_

Also, AI can track **learning from feedback**: if client feedback is recorded (maybe via support or closing notes), it can correlate. _"Projects where 'prep work' task was rushed had lower client satisfaction. Ensure adequate time for prep in future."_ Or simply note patterns like one team member consistently finishing tasks faster - maybe let them mentor others or take lead on critical tasks.

From a collaborative view, if multiple network members execute similar projects, AI could benchmark: _"Your average house painting project takes 10 days; network average is 8 days. Perhaps learn techniques or tools that peers use to be more efficient."_

**Collaborative Network Features:** Project collaboration in a network might involve joint projects or sharing skilled labor. For instance, a larger construction job in the community might be too big for one small contractor, so multiple network members form a temporary joint venture. TOSS can allow a **Project** to have tasks assigned to different companies in the network. Each company sees tasks relevant to them and can manage their parts (with limited access to others if needed). The central project coordinator (perhaps the network admin or lead contractor) sees the whole picture and each member's progress. This way, a project is truly collaborative - e.g., one business handles foundation, another handles structure, another does finishing. TOSS can integrate with accounting too: each member could bill the lead for their portion, and the lead bills the client - all tracked under one project.

Networks can also maintain a **pool of experts or volunteers** that can be assigned to projects. For example, if one member needs an extra electrician for a project, they could create a task and assign it to "Network Electrician Pool" - which notifies available electricians in the network (maybe who marked themselves free). The first to accept gets assigned and becomes responsible. TOSS could facilitate this by showing open tasks to users in a network job board.

For community projects (like building a community center via a business network's social initiative), multiple businesses might contribute resources (material, labor). They can all log their contributions under the project in TOSS. The project report then shows total contributions, who provided what, etc. - useful for transparency and impact reporting.

The Project Templates can be shared network-wide for common projects (like a standard "Install solar panel system" project template if many members do similar projects, providing a best-practice task list for consistency).

Also, on a knowledge level, project post-mortems can be shared. One business might note "Project X: We experienced delay due to late material delivery from Supplier Y." If allowed, that note could be visible to others or fed into AI insights for them (like cautioning when planning similar project to factor that supplier's lead time or choose alternate supplier from network experiences).

Finally, the network can showcase completed projects collectively - for marketing, a network portfolio. TOSS could feed data (with permission) to a network website page listing projects done by members (pictures, scope, feedback). This shows the network's collective capability to take on large or numerous projects.

In essence, the Projects module, when extended beyond a single business, becomes a tool for **coordinated effort and mutual aid**. It allows a group of small entities to act in concert on bigger initiatives, share workload, and learn from each project, thereby taking on projects that individually they couldn't, and improving success rates across the board.

## HR (Human Resources & Payroll Module)

**Functional Overview & Purpose:** The HR module manages **employee information and basic HR processes** such as attendance, leave (time off), payroll, and perhaps recruitment. For small businesses, this module keeps track of the people working in the business - even if it's just a few employees or part-timers - and automates things like salary calculations or leave balance tracking. It ensures employees are paid correctly and on time, and that their leave or off days are accounted for. Essentially, it gives small employers some of the organizational benefits bigger companies have, in a simplified form[\[52\]](https://9t9it.com/eprnext-faq#:~:text=What%20features%20are%20available%20in,the%20HR%20module%20of%20ERPNext).

**Core Workflows:**

- **Employee Records:** Create an **Employee** profile for each staff member (or contractor) once they join. This contains personal details (contact info, ID), job information (designation/role, date of joining, maybe salary details), and perhaps bank account for salary transfer. For example, if Thabo hires an assistant, he enters them as an Employee with start date and wage.
- **Attendance Tracking:** If needed, record **Attendance** daily or monthly. In a tiny shop, the owner might not formally track attendance; but if they want, they can mark each employee Present/Absent for each day. This can be done manually or even via an attendance code scan or mobile punch-in. It's optional, but it feeds into payroll if used (e.g., daily wage calculation or detecting unpaid leave days).
- **Leave Management:** Handle **Leave Applications** - when an employee requests a day off (sick leave, annual leave, etc.), they or the owner enters it into TOSS[\[54\]](https://9t9it.com/eprnext-faq#:~:text=How%20does%20ERPNext%20track%20employee,attendance). The system keeps track of how many leave days each employee has taken and has remaining (based on a leave policy you set, like 12 days per year). The owner can approve or reject leave requests. Approved leave can automatically mark those days as Absent (On Leave) in attendance to avoid double entry.
- **Payroll Processing:** Run **Payroll** at whatever frequency (monthly for most). TOSS can generate a **Payslip** for each employee[\[55\]](https://9t9it.com/eprnext-faq#:~:text=Can%20ERPNext%20handle%20payroll%20processing%3F). It will take their base salary or daily wage, add any allowances or deductions, and compute net pay. For example, it will automatically deduct for any unpaid absent days or add overtime if recorded, and calculate taxes if applicable (though for many small jobs taxes may not apply or be very simple). The owner reviews the payslips and then "submits" them, which posts the salary expense to accounting and sets an amount payable to the employee.
- **Salary Payments:** After finalizing payslips, record that you paid the salaries (if via cash, or bank transfer). This can be done by a **Payment Entry** to each employee or a batch "Bank Entry" if paying all at once. TOSS can mark the payslip as paid so you know who still needs to be paid. If integrated with a banking API, it could even initiate transfers, but likely not in MVP.
- **Expense Claims:** If employees spend on behalf of the company (e.g., buy stationery out of pocket), they can file an **Expense Claim** with receipts. The owner can approve and reimburse them through payroll or separately. This ensures those expenses are logged.
- **Optional - Recruitment:** If the business grows, an HR module could handle posting job openings and tracking applicants. Probably overkill for most small scenarios; not a focus unless needed later.
- **HR Reports & Documents:** Keep records like contracts, ID copies, etc., attached to employee records. Possibly generate standardized documents (like an offer letter or payslip PDF). Also reports such as who took how many sick days, etc.

**Business Rules & Validation:**

- **Unique Employee ID:** Each employee has an ID or number. TOSS ensures no duplicate (e.g., two records for same person). Possibly a rule like one user/employee link if employees login to system (less likely in micro business).
- **Leave Balance Enforcement:** When approving a leave application, the system checks if the employee has enough leave balance for that leave type. If not, it can either deduct as unpaid or reject application, depending on policy.
- **Payroll Dependency on Attendance:** If using attendance, payroll calculation can be configured to use it. For example, if someone is daily wage or hourly, TOSS will sum their present days or hours. If someone has unpaid absences, it will prorate salary. So the rule: ensure attendance and leaves are marked before running payroll, otherwise payslip might overpay. The system might warn if generating a payslip while some attendance records are missing or unapproved leave exists.
- **Tax and Statutory Deductions:** If the business is registered for PAYE (income tax) or UIF (unemployment insurance) etc., the module can calculate those based on formulas/slabs. The rule is to use latest tax tables configured. For very small salaries under tax thresholds, it'll just skip tax. The system should also not deduct things like pension or medical unless set up. Simpler: if the payroll is straightforward (just wages, maybe one flat % tax), we configure that and TOSS applies it.
- **Payslip Approval:** Optionally, one might require a second person to approve payslips before payment. In micro setups, the owner both prepares and approves, so that might be redundant.
- **Confidentiality:** Salary info should ideally only be visible to the owner or authorized HR role, not all employees. If employees have access to self-service (like to apply leave or view their own payslip), TOSS ensures they can't see others' data.
- **Accruals & Year-End:** If not needed in cash-accounting small biz skip, but in background it might accrue salary expense to the month even if paid next month, etc., if books are kept by accrual. Possibly not a focus here.
- **Employee Exit:** If someone leaves, mark them as left on a certain date. TOSS then should exclude them from future payroll runs automatically and make their record inactive. Also it could compute any leave encashment or final pay if that's in scope.

**Required Data Models & Key Fields:**

- **Employee:** Fields - Employee ID, Full Name, Date of Birth, Gender, Contact info, Address, Date of Joining, Department (if any), Designation/Role, Employment Type (permanent/contract), Current Status (Active/Left), and perhaps a Leave Policy link and Salary Structure link.
- **Salary Structure/Payslip:** In ERPNext, you define a Salary Structure (components of pay) and then generate Payslips. For simplicity, TOSS might just store salary info in Employee (like monthly salary or hourly rate) and generate a payslip on the fly. But structure allows complexities (basic, allowances, etc.). Fields for Payslip - Employee, Period (month), components table (each entry: component name, amount, whether addition/deduction), Gross, Deductions, Net Pay, and status Paid/Unpaid[\[55\]](https://9t9it.com/eprnext-faq#:~:text=Can%20ERPNext%20handle%20payroll%20processing%3F).
- **Attendance:** Fields - Employee, Date, Status (Present/Absent/Half-Day/On Leave), maybe check-in/out times if capturing hours.
- **Leave Type:** Fields - Name (Annual Leave, Sick Leave, etc.), total days allowed, whether carry forward allowed, whether is paid or not.
- **Leave Allocation:** Could define per employee how many days of each leave they get per year. Or use a default if all get same. Fields - Employee, Leave Type, Period (year), Allocated Days.
- **Leave Application:** Fields - Employee, Leave Type, From Date, To Date, Reason, Status (Applied/Pending, Approved, Rejected). The system calculates number of days.
- **Expense Claim:** Fields - Employee, details of expenses (could be child table of items with amount), total, status (to reimburse or paid).
- **Holiday List:** Possibly define public holidays so attendance auto-marks or not considered absences. Could default a country holiday list if needed.
- **Payroll Settings:** Could include things like work days per month (for pro-rate), default accounts for salary booking, etc.

**User Interface & Experience:** The HR module, being sensitive, might not be used by all employees on the system except for maybe a self-service portal for leave. The owner or manager will use it to run payroll and manage leave.

On the **Employee List**, you see all employees with maybe status and key info. Clicking one shows their profile - which can have tabs like Personal Info, Salary Info (e.g., monthly salary, maybe stored as a component), Leave Balances (display each type and remaining days).

For attendance, if used, the UI could be a simple calendar grid: each employee as row and dates as columns, where you tick P/A. Or the employee can have an "Attendance" form for each day. Might be easier: a daily attendance form where owner selects date, and then marks each employee present or absent in a list and saves. TOSS auto-fills defaults (everyone present unless ticked absent, for example).

The **Leave Application** UI for an employee (if allowed self-service) would be a simple form: pick dates, type, reason, submit. The owner then sees a notification or a list of leave requests to approve. Approving one automatically updates that employee's leave balance and can mark those days in attendance as leave (not counted as absence for payroll).

**Payroll** UI should be simple: likely a wizard or a button "Process Payroll for \[Month\]". The system will gather each active employee, calculate their earnings/deductions. The owner can review a summary (like a table of employees vs net pay) and click each if detail needed. Then finalize all payslips with one action. After that, a step to "Make Payments" - perhaps listing all payslips with option to create a Bank Advice or mark as paid one by one. If it's cash salaries, maybe the owner clicks "Mark all as Paid (Cash)" after physically paying them.

Payslips can be printed or emailed to employees. The format should be clean, showing gross, each deduction, net, and employer info. TOSS can generate these PDFs.

The **Reports** include e.g., a Leave Balance report (who has how many days left), an Attendance report, payroll register (list of all pay amounts for a period). Possibly compliance reports if needed, like a summary to fill on statutory forms.

**Offline Capabilities:** HR tasks like running payroll likely will be done online in office. But what about, say, marking attendance on a job site? Possibly a supervisor could tick attendance offline on their device and sync later. Or an employee could apply leave offline (not likely unless app is used in no-signal area, but could happen). These are not high-frequency urgent actions, so offline support is not critical but nice to have.

If offline, the request would just queue and send on connectivity. For attendance, if offline, one could mark present, and when sync occurs, it populates the central record. Usually there's no conflict unless two people marked something differently (which wouldn't happen unless the owner and supervisor both independently mark attendance, in which case one will override - but small teams likely coordinate that).

Payslips likely require connection to fetch latest data and push to accounts, so that's an online activity. If the owner had to run payroll without net, maybe they could do it offline and print locally, but syncing to accounting would wait until online.

**Example Use Case (Township Context):** \*Amina runs a small salon with 3 employees: two hairdressers and one assistant. She uses HR module to manage them. At the start, she created Employee records for each with their join date and monthly wage. The salon is closed on Mondays and none works that day, so she configured a Holiday List for Mondays and public holidays so those don't count as absences.

Every morning, she quickly notes attendance. The default setting in TOSS marks everyone Present by default; if someone is out, she opens that day in the Attendance tool and marks that employee Absent. Last Tuesday, her assistant, Thandi, called in sick. Amina opened TOSS and created a **Leave Application** for Thandi - from Tuesday to Wednesday as sick leave (two days)[\[54\]](https://9t9it.com/eprnext-faq#:~:text=How%20does%20ERPNext%20track%20employee,attendance). She marked it as Approved. TOSS deducted those from Thandi's sick leave balance (she gets 5 sick days per year; now 3 left) and tagged those two days so payroll won't pay them (Amina's policy is unpaid sick days for part-timers).

At end of the month, Amina runs **Payroll**. She clicks "Process Payroll for March". TOSS generates payslips: each hairdresser at R5,000 (no absences, so full pay), Thandi's pay is usually R3,000 but the system sees 2 unpaid sick days (it knows from attendance/leave) and deducts R200 (say her wage ~R100/day) so shows R2,800 gross[\[55\]](https://9t9it.com/eprnext-faq#:~:text=Can%20ERPNext%20handle%20payroll%20processing%3F). It also calculates a small tax for one hairdresser who is above tax threshold (let's say R50 tax).

She reviews and then submits the payslips. Accounting entries are posted (debit salary expense ~R12,800, credit salary payable). She then records **Payment**: she pays cash, so she marks all as paid from the business cash account (TOSS could let her do this in one go or one by one). Now the liability clears and cash is reduced accordingly in accounts.

Each employee gets their payslip via WhatsApp (she exported PDF from TOSS mobile and sent it, or if they had email, email). They can see clearly their earnings and any deductions. Thandi sees 2 days unpaid noted, which matches their earlier discussion.

Over time, Amina uses reports like "Monthly Attendance" to see patterns - maybe one staff has frequent Mondays off beyond holidays. She might counsel them about reliability. The Leave Balance report at year-end shows one hairdresser didn't use any leave; she encourages her to take a break (to avoid burnout).

Because payroll data is in TOSS, when Amina needed income verification for a loan, she quickly pulled a **Payroll Register** report for last 6 months, demonstrating she pays salaries regularly - this helped her credibility.

In the network context, if her salon staff wanted to join a collective insurance plan, having formal payslips and records from TOSS made it easy to prove employment and income. Also, if a staff member works at two shops in the network (maybe part-time at each), the HR module could handle that by two employee entries or a shared one if network allowed linking - but at least records exist.

For Amina, HR module took away the headache of remembering who took leave or how to prorate pay - TOSS handled it, and employees are confident in the accuracy of their pay.\*

**SaaS 2.0 - AI & Insights:** AI in HR acts like a smart HR assistant. It can watch patterns in attendance and suggest improvements or alerts. For example, _"Thandi has taken 5 sick days in 2 months, higher than average - check if there are underlying issues or if help is needed."_ Or _"Lebo often works overtime on Saturdays - ensure she's compensated or consider adjusting staffing on weekends."_

AI can also assist with **scheduling**. An example: based on sales patterns or footfall (from POS data), AI might suggest optimal staffing: _"Fridays are very busy in the salon; consider having an extra helper on Fridays (maybe part-time) to improve customer service."_ Conversely, _"Wednesdays are slow - you might not need both hairdressers full-day, could rotate or have one focus on marketing."_ This crosses HR with sales insights.

For leave management, AI might forecast workload vs staff availability. If many staff request overlap leave, it can warn: _"Two employees requested leave on the same week next month - your available workforce will be 1, which might be insufficient given last month's bookings. You may need to decline or hire temp help."_

On performance, if any KPI data is integrated (like sales per stylist), AI could highlight who's performing well or needing support, which in HR context might influence training or rewards: _"Stylist A's services bring in 30% more revenue than peers - consider bonus or asking them to share techniques."_ or _"Stylist B has lower customer retention (from CRM feedback) - perhaps provide additional training."_

AI can also help ensure compliance: reminding about things like _"It's been a year, time to renew Worker X's contract"_ or _"Check if all employees' certifications (first aid, etc.) are up to date."_ If any labour regulations (like minimum wage changes or leave accrual rules) change and are input, AI might notify the owner to adjust salaries or structures accordingly.

For payroll, AI could verify anomalies: _"This month's total payroll is 20% higher than last - did you add staff or might there be an error?"_ or detect if someone's pay is consistently docked for overtime/absences, to prompt a conversation.

**Collaborative Network Features:** In a network, HR could be leveraged for **shared talent pools** and **benefit programs**. For instance, the network might maintain a centralized roster of vetted part-time or fill-in workers (accountants, drivers, etc.) who can be temporarily employed by any member. Through TOSS, a business could request an extra hand; the network HR could then assign someone (turning them into an "Employee" of that business for a day or handle payment centrally). The system would ensure that worker's total hours across the network are tracked so they aren't over-committed. Payment could be done by the network central and then billed to the business, or directly by the hiring business - TOSS is flexible but facilitates these records.

Another angle is **collective bargaining** for employees' welfare. If the network uses TOSS to aggregate HR data, they know how many people are employed in total and maybe their salary ranges. They could approach an insurance for group health cover. TOSS could then manage the deductions from payroll for those who opt in. For example, if 50 employees across network join a medical scheme, each one's payroll in TOSS will have a deduction line (and a central account in network books pays the insurer).

The network might also run **training sessions** (like customer service training or safety training). Through TOSS HR, they can schedule and track attendance for these among member employees. Employee records could have certifications listed, so the network can quickly see who from any member has a certain certification if a project needs it.

Additionally, if an employee moves between businesses in the network (common in informal sectors - e.g., a good cashier might go from Shop A to manage Shop B opened by another member), having their performance history or basic info portable can help. With permission, an employee's record (sans confidential bits) could be shared to the new employer in the network. Essentially, a mini reference that's verified through the network's HR. The network might thus build a reputation system for workers: reliable staff could find jobs with other member businesses easily (like an internal LinkedIn). The HR module could have a flag or note visible to network HR admins: e.g., "This cashier was trained on POS and had excellent attendance at Shop A."

For compliance and advocacy, the network can use aggregated HR data to ensure fair labor practices. If one business is underpaying below minimum wage, the network might intervene (assuming open sharing or an audit). Or the network can negotiate group rates for payroll services, retirement funds, etc., using the power of numbers. TOSS providing clear, aggregate stats (e.g., "Our network employs 200 people with total monthly payroll of R500k") strengthens their position in such negotiations or in representing the informal sector's employment contribution in discussions with authorities.

In summary, while each business manages its own employees day-to-day, the network can uplift the quality of employment by pooling resources - from training to insurance - and TOSS HR gives the data backbone to do that fairly and efficiently, all while protecting individual businesses' confidentiality where needed.

## Support (Helpdesk & Customer Support Module)

**Functional Overview & Purpose:** The Support module provides a system for **managing customer issues and inquiries**. It allows businesses to log customer complaints or requests (often called tickets or issues), track their status, and ensure timely resolution. Even small businesses benefit from a lightweight helpdesk: for instance, a small electronics shop can track warranty repair cases, or a co-op can handle support requests from members. This module helps maintain good customer service by making sure every query is recorded and addressed and by building a knowledge base of solutions for common problems[\[57\]](https://zikpro.frappe.cloud/ERPNext-Helpdesk#:~:text=,manage%20tickets%20by%20connecting%20your).

**Core Workflows:**

- **Issue/Ticket Logging:** When a customer reaches out with a problem (product defect, service complaint, question), create a **Support Issue** in TOSS[\[57\]](https://zikpro.frappe.cloud/ERPNext-Helpdesk#:~:text=,manage%20tickets%20by%20connecting%20your). Capture the customer, date, and details of the issue. Issues can come via many channels: phone calls (manually logged), emails (could be auto-converted to issues if integrated), website form (feeding into support), or walk-ins (staff logs it).
- **Issue Assignment & Categorization:** Categorize the issue (e.g., _Billing_, _Technical_, _General Inquiry_) and assign it to the person or team responsible[\[57\]](https://zikpro.frappe.cloud/ERPNext-Helpdesk#:~:text=,manage%20tickets%20by%20connecting%20your). In a tiny business, that might always be the owner or a specific employee. You can set priority (Low/Med/High/Urgent) depending on severity (e.g., "Fridge not cooling - urgent if under warranty"), so urgent ones are addressed first.
- **Communication & Resolution:** Use the issue to track all communications. You can reply to the customer (if via email, TOSS can send emails and capture their replies under the issue), or note that you called them and what was discussed[\[91\]](https://zikpro.frappe.cloud/ERPNext-Helpdesk#:~:text=,important%20and%20urgent%20they%20are). The aim is to keep a full history. As you work on the problem, update the status: e.g., _"Identified cause - part needs replacement, awaiting part"_ (maybe a custom status On Hold), then _"Resolved"_ once fixed. Possibly the customer can be notified at each stage automatically.
- **Service Level Agreements (SLAs):** If you promise customers certain response/resolve times, configure those. The system will then monitor each ticket against the SLA (using timestamps)[\[60\]](https://zikpro.frappe.cloud/ERPNext-Helpdesk#:~:text=,that%20have%20already%20been%20set). If a ticket is breaching its SLA (e.g., not responded within 24h for high priority), it can escalate - maybe by highlighting in red or notifying a manager. In a small biz, escalation might just mean it bugs the owner with an extra alert.
- **Knowledge Base & FAQs:** Over time, build a **Knowledge Base** of common questions and their answers or known fixes[\[61\]](https://zikpro.frappe.cloud/ERPNext-Helpdesk#:~:text=,tickets%20by%20connecting%20your%20email). Support agents can link an issue to a Knowledge Base article or quickly search it to answer. Possibly, this can be exposed on the website for customers to self-help. For example, an article "How to troubleshoot a fridge that's not cooling" that customers can read instead of calling.
- **Maintenance Visits/Service:** If support involves physical service (like sending a technician), the Support module can schedule a **Maintenance Visit**. That might be integrated with Projects or a calendar. E.g., for a warranty repair, create a visit record under the issue with date and who will go. Once done, close it.
- **Feedback & Closure:** After resolving an issue, you might capture customer feedback (rating or comment). The module could send a quick "Was your issue resolved satisfactorily?" or just mark as closed. Good to gather if customers are happy, and if not, maybe reopen or escalate further.

**Business Rules & Validation:**

- **Unique Ticket IDs:** Each issue gets an auto ID (like SUP-1001) to reference easily. If a customer calls about the same issue, support should use the same ticket rather than creating duplicates - search by customer or by keywords helps avoid duplication.
- **Customer Link:** Ideally every issue is linked to a Customer record (or at least a name and contact captured). This way, one can see all issues that customer had historically, which helps in understanding their needs or frustration level.
- **Status Flow:** Usually, New → Open → Resolved → Closed. The system enforces some flows: e.g., you shouldn't close without marking resolved or having a resolution note. Or if reopened, status goes back to Open. Each transition might capture time stamps (for SLA calculations).
- **Time Logging:** If needed, record time spent on tickets (could integrate with timesheets if billing for support or just to track workload). This might be heavy for a microbiz unless they bill clients for service time.
- **SLA timers:** The system should automatically start timers when ticket is logged. If "Response SLA = 4 hours", once 4 hours passes with no agent response posted, it flags escalation. Similarly, a "Resolve SLA = 24 hours" would count down from open to closed. Holidays and off-hours can be configured to not count if needed (maybe not for microbiz, but the capability exists).
- **Priority Handling:** If a ticket is marked Urgent, maybe the system alerts the support person immediately (SMS or special sound) versus low priority might just sit in queue. Should be visual indicators as well.
- **Knowledge Base Quality:** Possibly require that if an issue is recurring, a KB article is created. But small scale maybe not needed. At least ensure KB articles are reviewed and approved (so customers don't see incorrect info). Perhaps each article has a status draft/published.
- **Customer Updates:** After any meaningful change, ideally the customer should be informed. The system can send an email or text based on triggers (e.g., "Your support ticket #123 has been resolved. If you're satisfied, let us know."). Ensure that the communication uses simple language.
- **Integration with Inventory/Service:** If resolving an issue required issuing a spare part from inventory or creating a sales invoice (if out of warranty and chargeable), the system should allow linking that. E.g., you open an issue, decide it needs a replacement part, you can create a Delivery Note or Sales Invoice from the issue to bill that part, and it's all linked. The rule is to capture any financial impact of support in the system.
- **Privacy:** Keep any sensitive info (like a customer's logs or attachments) accessible only to authorized users, in case multiple people use the system. Also comply if a customer asks to delete their data (POPIA/GDPR compliance) - typically uncommon in such context but good practice.

**Required Data Models & Key Fields:**

- **Issue/Ticket:** Fields - Issue ID, Subject, Description (the problem detail), Customer (link or at least name/phone), Status, Priority, Type/Category, Assigned To (user handling it), Opening Date, Response Deadline (if SLA), Resolve Deadline, and fields to record Response Time and Resolve Time actual. Could also have an Impact or Product field if needed (e.g., which product was this about).
- **Issue Communication:** Each ticket would have a timeline of communications. Likely stored as a Communication doctype with fields: Issue ID (link), Date, Sender/Recipient, Medium (Email/Phone/Note), Content (message text or call summary), and perhaps an indicator if this satisfies first response.
- **Service Level Agreement (SLA):** Possibly a doctype or config where you define rules: e.g., Priority = High → Respond in 2 hrs, Resolve in 8 hrs. It can also have business hours defined. The issue picks an SLA based on category or customer maybe.
- **Maintenance Visit:** If needed, a doctype with Issue link, Assigned Employee, Date, and a status (Scheduled, Completed) plus a report/notes field to fill what was done.
- **Knowledge Base Article (Solution):** Fields - Title, Content (rich text with images possibly), Category (FAQ, Troubleshooting, etc.), Issue Link (if it was created from a specific issue), Published (yes/no). Possibly a separate portal for customers to browse these.
- **Feedback/Rating:** Could be a simple child of Issue or a field: like Rating 1-5, Comments. Might be filled by agent after asking customer or via a form link the customer gets.

**User Interface & Experience:** The Support interface prominently shows open tickets. A small business might have just a handful at a time, but it's key none get forgotten. The **Issue List** could be by default filtered to Open tickets, showing columns like ID, Subject, Customer, Priority, Age (time since opened), and who's assigned. Overdue ones might be highlighted or sorted up.

When clicking an issue, you see the full details and a conversation thread. Much like a email client or chat: the customer query at top, then support's response, etc., in order. The agent can type a reply in a rich text box if emailing out (with option to pick a solution from KB to insert). Or if the customer is on phone, the agent can instead click "Add Comment" and note what was discussed or promised.

To speed up solving, a sidebar could show related knowledge base suggestions (e.g., search knowledge base by issue keywords)[\[61\]](https://zikpro.frappe.cloud/ERPNext-Helpdesk#:~:text=,tickets%20by%20connecting%20your%20email). The agent might just copy-paste an answer from an article to reply if it fits.

There may also be canned actions: "Mark as Resolved" button which prompts to add a resolution note and set status = Resolved in one go. If the process is the owner usually takes care of all, they might not even assign, but just use status and priority.

If the business has a website, a simplified **Support Form** can be embedded, which on submission creates an Issue in TOSS. The owner sees it and responds via TOSS sending an email.

Support module could integrate with WhatsApp via Twilio or similar - but for MVP probably not. Likely use email as primary digital channel, plus manual logging for calls.

**Offline Capabilities:** Support agents (like the owner or a staff) might get calls when not connected to the system. They can still note down the issue and later create the ticket in TOSS (backdated creation time). There's not much need for real-time offline usage except maybe if on-site service where no internet, they can open the ticket info offline (e.g., check knowledge base while at client site). If they update it offline (like closing a ticket after fixing on site), it syncs later so central records update.

If SMS/WhatsApp integration is considered, offline won't send those - they'd queue until online.

**Example Use Case (Township Context):** \*Sello runs a small electronics repair shop and sells appliances. He uses Support module to track warranty and repair cases.

One of his customers, Naledi, bought a fridge 3 months ago and now it's not cooling. Naledi calls Sello. Sello opens TOSS Support on his laptop and **creates a new Issue**: "Fridge not cooling - Naledi Dube"[\[57\]](https://zikpro.frappe.cloud/ERPNext-Helpdesk#:~:text=,manage%20tickets%20by%20connecting%20your). He fills in her phone number and selects category "Product Defect" and marks priority as High (since a fridge issue is urgent due to food spoilage). He assigns it to himself (by default).

He tells Naledi he'll send a technician. He then clicks "Maintenance Visit" on the ticket and schedules a visit for that afternoon. The system captures that as an appointment associated with the issue, and he sets the issue status to "In Progress" (since it's being handled). If he had an SLA (say respond within 1hr, resolve in 1 day for warranty issues), the system would count - in this case, he responded immediately by phone, so he logs a note "Spoke to Naledi, scheduled technician today 3pm" - which satisfies the response SLA[\[60\]](https://zikpro.frappe.cloud/ERPNext-Helpdesk#:~:text=,that%20have%20already%20been%20set).

His technician, Thabo, goes to Naledi's place, checks the fridge. The cause: a faulty thermostat. Thabo has a spare part. He replaces it on site. He updates Sello via WhatsApp, and back in the system, Sello opens the issue and records an Internal Note: "Thermostat replaced by Thabo on-site, fridge cooling now." He sets the status to **Resolved**. This triggers TOSS to send an SMS to Naledi: "Your issue #FR-1001 has been resolved. If you need further help, contact us." (Assuming he set up an SMS integration for notifications).

Naledi calls the next day to confirm it's working fine, and thanks him. Sello then **closes** the ticket on TOSS. He also has TOSS record that this was a warranty case - no charge to customer, but he does log under the ticket's costs the thermostat part (R200 from his stock). That way, he can later see how many warranty parts he's giving out.

Over time, Sello notices several fridge issues of the same model for thermostat failure. Using Support's simple reports, he sees 5 similar tickets in two months. That's a pattern. He writes a **Knowledge Base article**: "Fridge Model X not cooling - thermostat issue" describing the symptom and fix[\[61\]](https://zikpro.frappe.cloud/ERPNext-Helpdesk#:~:text=,tickets%20by%20connecting%20your%20email). This helps him or his staff handle future cases faster (and he might press the supplier for better parts or compensation). If customers check his website's help section, they might see this article too and realize it's a known issue (which might either reassure them it can be fixed or push the supplier to recall).

If Sello's business was part of a network, he could also share these support patterns. E.g., other electronics shops in network also log fridge issues - together they compile a **common solution database** and collectively approach the fridge manufacturer about the defect, using data of how many cases occurred (TOSS can generate that report easily from issues across network members).

For now, Sello is just happy that nothing falls through: every customer issue is tracked, even if he can't fix it immediately, it's on his list. His customers are happier because they see he has a system - they get updates and their problems aren't forgotten in the busy shuffle.\*

**SaaS 2.0 - AI & Insights:** AI in Support can significantly improve response quality and speed. One main feature is **suggested solutions**: as soon as an issue is logged (or as the agent types the complaint), the AI can search the knowledge base and past tickets for similar keywords[\[61\]](https://zikpro.frappe.cloud/ERPNext-Helpdesk#:~:text=,tickets%20by%20connecting%20your%20email). It might pop up: _"Likely cause: Thermostat failure - see Solution #212"_. This saves Sello's time in figuring out the problem. If integrated with something like GPT, it could even draft a tailored answer to the customer based on the knowledge base.

AI can also categorize and prioritize tickets automatically (NLP). E.g., if a message says "fridge not working and food spoiling, very upset!", the AI could label it Urgent and type "Product Defect" without Sello manually doing it. It might even detect sentiment (customer is angry)[\[91\]](https://zikpro.frappe.cloud/ERPNext-Helpdesk#:~:text=,important%20and%20urgent%20they%20are) and alert Sello to handle carefully.

For SLA, AI can forecast if a ticket is at risk of breaching. It knows Sello's usual resolution times and backlog. It might warn: _"You have 5 open high-priority issues and typically resolve 2 per day; some may breach SLA without extra help."_ This could prompt him to maybe call in a freelance tech from network for a day.

AI could facilitate a **self-service chatbot**. If a customer goes to Sello's website, a bot could ask what's wrong, then pull answers from knowledge base to help them (using an NLP model). For instance, _Customer:_ "My fridge isn't cold." _Bot:_ "Is the light on? \[Yes\] Have you tried adjusting thermostat? \[Yes\] It might be a thermostat failure, which we can repair under warranty. Please contact us for a service visit."_ If the bot can't help, it logs an issue for a human follow-up. This reduces trivial tickets.

Also, across the network, AI can identify product issues trending. E.g., _"15 issues about Model X fridge not cooling across the network in last month (5 yours, 10 others). Likely a widespread defect_[_\[51\]_](https://github.com/Toss-Online-Services/TossErp/blob/10c8061dab87e2b12429c455fe4113e62a69f8e0/toss-web/server/data/modules.ts#L360-L364)_."_ It might alert all network support agents or suggest proactively reaching out to customers who bought that model (sales records can be cross-referenced) to offer a fix before it fails - turning support into a preventive service and good customer care.

AI can optimize support scheduling too: if multiple service visits in the same area come in, it can group them for Thabo on one trip (like a route optimization for field service).

Finally, AI monitors customer satisfaction. If a particular issue type is taking too long to resolve on average or certain customers have repeated issues, it surfaces that: _"Customer Naledi had 3 issues in 3 months - maybe follow up for product replacement or goodwill gesture."_ Or _"Tickets about microwave ovens often reopen - check if initial solutions are effective."_

**Collaborative Network Features:** Support is an area where collaboration can shine, especially for knowledge sharing and load balancing:

- **Shared Knowledge Base:** The network can maintain a central KB that all members contribute to and use. Suppose there are many small electronics shops; they all log their support issues and solutions. Over time, a rich repository of problems and fixes is built (like "common TV faults and fixes"). New or less experienced members benefit from this collective wisdom when they encounter issues - they search the network KB and find solutions discovered by others. TOSS can handle permissions so certain internal notes may remain private if desired, but generally everyone gains.
- **Specialist Escalation:** If one member can't solve an issue (maybe they lack advanced diagnostic tools), they could escalate it to a network specialist. In TOSS, that could mean transferring the ticket to another company (like how one might escalate to tier-2 support). For instance, Shop A gets a complex computer repair issue they can't fix, they escalate the ticket to Shop B's expert. The ticket in TOSS would change owner (and possibly company context). Shop B fixes it and either returns the item or instructs Shop A. The network might have an agreed billing for such help. TOSS would allow linking this to an inter-company invoice possibly (Shop B charges Shop A for the service at an agreed rate). But to the customer, it's seamless - their issue gets solved, via network cooperation.
- **Network Ticket Pool:** If one support center is swamped, the network could have an overflow mechanism. For example, an online marketplace run by the network fields customer queries about orders. Those tickets could be answered by any available agent across the network, not just one central team. In TOSS, a ticket could be visible in a "network queue" where multiple support agents from different members can pick and resolve them (especially if they are generic questions or follow-ups). This is kind of cross-company task assignment, which the platform could allow through a shared support space.
- **Unified Customer Support Experience:** The network may present a unified front for support. E.g., "Township Tech Support Line" for all electronics purchased from any member. Customers call one number, tickets are logged, and then TOSS routes it to the specific seller or a network helpdesk. From a customer view, it's one entity, but internally, TOSS knows which company is responsible and assigns accordingly. This requires integration of data so the helpdesk can see sale records to verify warranty etc., which a network central system could query from members' data if allowed.
- **Collective SLA Commitment:** The network might promise certain service levels (like all members in the network guarantee 24h response to complaints). TOSS can help monitor each member's compliance. If someone is lagging, network leadership can intervene or assist (maybe they need training or resources). It keeps the whole network's reputation up when everyone meets the standard.
- **Cost Sharing & Bulk Contracts:** For example, network could have a deal with a courier for handling returns or field service logistics. Support module knows how many on-site visits or returns happened network-wide (from Maintenance Visit entries). They could collectively negotiate better rates for those with a courier.
- **Community Support Initiatives:** Perhaps the network runs a community helpdesk for certain services (like tech advice clinics on weekends). TOSS support could track those inquiries too, giving insight into community needs that might inform business opportunities.

In conclusion, the Support module in a network context not only improves each business's customer service but binds them into a support ecosystem where knowledge and effort is shared. It ensures small businesses can provide reliable, efficient support on par with bigger companies, by collectively leveraging their strengths and learning, thereby boosting customer confidence and satisfaction across the board.
